The Qadariyya as Magians: First Point of Similarity
Maturidi cites the hadith and explains the first similarity between Qadariyya and Magians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the hadith 'Qadariyya are the Magians of this Ummah' to expose a fundamental similarity between the dualism of the Magians and the Mu'tazilite doctrine of an originated will.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya share the Magian error of attributing cosmic causation to an unoriginated entity besides God.

Imam Maturidi begins by noting the consensus among kalam scholars in condemning the label 'Qadariyya', yet everyone disavows it. The Prophet's hadith pointing to the Magians provides the key to understanding the true referent. In this passage, Maturidi explains the first of several aspects of similarity, focusing on the origination of a secondary principle (thought or will) that challenges God's exclusive sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without deep analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the phrase 'Magians of this Ummah' and how Maturidi builds on it.
2
Identify the hadith and its purpose
Find the hadith and note that Maturidi uses it to clarify who truly deserves the dispraised name 'Qadariyya'.
3
Understand the Magian belief
Trace the Magian narrative: God originally alone, then a bad thought, leading to Iblis and the dualism of good and evil.
Observe the claim that God had no power over evil and Iblis no power over good.
4
See the Mu'tazilite parallel
Maturidi compares the Magian 'bad thought' to the Mu'tazilite 'originated will', showing the same structure but with a different name.
Note that the Mu'tazila attribute the entire world to this will, making them more excessive.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why both positions are blameworthy, and why the Mu'tazila are even more excessive in this error.
This reinforces the importance of affirming God's complete sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في ذم القدرية أو المعتزلة. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: أجمع أهل الكلام على ذم اسم القدرية، وتبرأ كل منهم عنه. وقد روى في ذلك عن رسول الله ما يمكن به السبيل إلى معرفة من له حقيقة هذا الاسم، وهو قوله: «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة». ومعلوم أنه أراد به ذم أهلها بمعنى شاركوا فيه المجوس فيما خالف به المجوس أهل الأديان من القول. فلا بد من تأمل ذلك ليظهر حقيقة أهل هذا الاسم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وكان الأصل الذي ذُمَّ به المجوس مما خالفوا به أهل الأديان من أوجه. أحدها أنهم قالوا كان الله واحداً لا شريك له. ثم حدثت منه فكرة رديئة، إما لما أصابته عينه، أو لما ظن أن يكون له عدو ينازعه. فإذا إبليس حدث من تلك الفكرة الرديئة؛ فخلق هو شر العالم، والله خيره، من غير أن كان الله قدرة على خلق شيء من الشر والفساد ونحو ذلك، أو لإبليس قدرة على خلق شيء من الخير والصلاح، فقام العالم بهما. وبهذا كله خالفوا أهل الأديان. ومعلوم أن هذا كله أوصاف ذم ونعوت شين. ثم للمعتزلة عن كل هذه الصفات نصيب، فلذلك لقبوا باسم القدرية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ووجه ذلك أن المعتزلة زعمت أن الله تعالى كان ولا شيء غيره، ثم حدثت الإرادة من غير أن كان من الله بحدوثها إرادة، أو اختيار منه إليها. أفيكون لهذا القول معنى سوى أن كانت فكان بها جميع العالم؟ إذ من قولهم: إن العالم فعل الله، وإنه كان باختيار، وإن الاختيار إرادة، كقوله: فعال لما يريد. قسمت المعتزلة تلك الحادثة إرادة والمجوس فكرة؛ وهي واحدة، بينهما اختلاف في الاسم لا في الحقيقة. ثم جعلت المجوس بها نصف العالم والمعتزلة كل العالم، فيكونان في الحاصل تحت قول ذميم، والمعتزلة زائدة.
Chapter on the dispraise of the Qadariyya or the Mu'tazila. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The people of kalam are agreed on dispraise of the name 'Qadariyya', and each of them disavows it. And there has been narrated concerning that from the Messenger of God what makes possible the way to knowledge of who truly deserves this name, namely his saying: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this Ummah.' And it is known that he meant by it the dispraise of its people in the sense that they share with the Magians in the statement by which the Magians opposed the people of religions. So it is necessary to reflect on that so that the reality of the people of this name becomes clear. And there is no power except with God. The basis by which the Magians were dispraised, opposing the people of religions in aspects, first: They said God was one, having no partner. Then there originated from Him a bad thought, either because His eye struck him, or because he thought that he had an enemy to contend with him. Then Iblis originated from that bad thought; so he created the evil of the world, and God created its good, without God having power to create anything of evil and corruption and the like, or Iblis having power to create anything of good and righteousness. So the world came about through both of them. By all this they opposed the people of religions. And it is known that all this are attributes of dispraise and ugly qualities. Then the Mu'tazila have a share in all these attributes, therefore they were called by the name Qadariyya. And there is no power except with God. The explanation of that is that the Mu'tazila claimed that God existed and nothing else, then will originated without God having will for its origination or choice towards it. So does this statement have any meaning other than that it was and through it the whole world came about? For it is from their statement: The world is the act of God, and it was by choice, and choice is will, as He says: 'Doer of what He wills.' The Mu'tazila divided that originated thing as will, and the Magians as thought; and it is the same, differing only in name not in reality. Then the Magians made by it half of the world and the Mu'tazila the whole world, so they both fall under a blameworthy saying, and the Mu'tazila are in excess.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. It begins by stating the consensus of kalam scholars in dispraising the name Qadariyya, and that everyone disavows it. Then it cites the hadith 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this Ummah' and notes that this dispraise is because they share with the Magians in what opposed the people of religions. Maturidi then explains the first aspect of similarity. The Magians claimed that God was originally one, but then a bad thought originated from Him, from which Iblis was created. Iblis then created the evil in the world, while God created the good. They held that God had no power to create evil, nor Iblis to create good. This dualism contradicts monotheistic religions. The Mu'tazila have a share in these blameworthy attributes because they also introduced an originated will (al-irada) that came to be without God having a prior will or choice for it. In effect, both Magians and Mu'tazila posit an originated entity (thought or will) as the basis for the world's coming into being. The difference is only in name, not reality. Then the Magians attribute only half the world to this entity (evil), while the Mu'tazila attribute the entire world to it. So both positions are blameworthy, with the Mu'tazila being even more excessive.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith 'Qadariyya are the Magians' refers only to extreme Qadariyya who deny predestination entirely.
Correction: Maturidi shows the hadith applies to the Mu'tazila as well, because they share the Magian error of positing an originated entity (will) without prior divine choice, embedded in their broader doctrine.

Checkpoint

B) Both posit an originated entity (thought or will) without prior divine choice or power, making it the source of part or all of the world.
C) Both deny God's power over good.
D) Both believe that evil comes from Satan.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila, by positing an originated will without divine prior will, replicate the Magian dualistic error in a more extreme form.

?

How does Maturidi's exposure of dualism in this lesson convict you to repent and affirm complete tawhid in your heart? What action will you take today to uproot that hidden shirk?
?
Maturidi shows that even the Mu'tazila — who claimed to uphold tawhid — fell into a subtle dualism by separating God's will from His eternal essence. Examine your own understanding: do you ever think of God's will, power, or knowledge as “things” that happen to Him or arise in Him? The Prophet's Censure of the Qadariyya: A Call to Examine Doctrine
Maturidi cites the hadith naming Qadariyya as Magians and explains its censure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and purpose of the Prophet's statement that the Qadariyya are the Magians of this nation, as explained by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
The Prophet's comparison is doctrinal censure, not mere insult—it demands reflection.

Imam Maturidi begins his discussion of the Qadariyya sect by citing a well-known hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) labels them the 'Magians of this nation.' This is not a casual insult but a serious doctrinal classification. Maturidi clarifies that the Prophet intended to blame them because they share a fundamental error with the Zoroastrians—opposing the core belief in divine predestination held by the people of revelation. The passage then calls for careful examination to uncover the true nature of those who bear this name, setting the stage for deeper theological analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi immediately identifies the hadith and then explains its purpose.
The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' is a common closing invocation.
2
Identify the three parts of the passage
The passage has three logical moves: (1) Citation of the hadith, (2) Clarification of its intent (blame for sharing in what Magians opposed), (3) Call for contemplation.
3
Reflect on why this comparison is significant
Ask: Why does Maturidi emphasize that the Qadariyya share with the Magians in 'that which they opposed the people of religions'? What core doctrine is at stake?
The shared error is likely the denial of divine predestination (qadar).

Source Text

الاسم، وهو قوله : «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة». ومعلوم أنه أراد به ذم أهلها بمعنى شاركوا فيه المجوس فيما خالف به المجوس أهل الأديان من القول. فلا بد من تأمل ذلك ليظهر حقيقة أهل هذا الاسم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

And it is known that he intended by it to blame its people in the sense that they shared with the Magians in that which the Magians opposed the people of religions in speech. So there is no avoiding contemplating that to make apparent the true reality of the people of this name. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage introduces a well-known hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) calls the Qadariyya (those who deny divine predestination) the 'Magians of this nation.' Maturidi clarifies that the Prophet intended censure because the Qadariyya share a core doctrinal error with the Zoroastrians (Magians)—namely, opposing the followers of revealed religions on a fundamental point. The author then calls for careful examination of this comparison to uncover the true nature of those who bear this name. The phrase 'there is no power except with Allah' concludes the introduction, signaling reliance on God.

Magian Dualism and the Mu'tazila Will: A Shared Error
Maturidi exposes the logical parallel between Magian dualism and Mu'tazila will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi parallels the Magian concept of a 'bad thought' with the Mu'tazila concept of an originated will, and why he considers both blameworthy.

Core Idea
A secondary origin for the world, whether called 'thought' or 'will', leads to dualism and contradicts tawhid.

Maturidi begins his critique by exposing a surprising parallel: the Magians' dualism and the Mu'tazila's view on the origin of will both posit a secondary principle alongside God. This may seem like a stretch, but the author carefully demonstrates that the two beliefs share the same logical structure. Let's trace his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Magian claim
Read how the Magians say a 'bad thought' arose from God, leading to Iblis who creates evil.
Note the dualistic consequence: God creates good, Iblis creates evil.
2
Note Maturidi's criticism
He states that this opposes all religions and is blameworthy.
3
Connect to Mu'tazila
Read the Mu'tazila claim: will occurred without prior will or choice from God.
This makes will a secondary originated entity.
4
See the parallel
Maturidi says both are one thing, differing only in name—thought vs. will.
Both become the source of the world's existence.
5
Conclude with Maturidi's judgment
The Mu'tazila are more blameworthy because they attribute the entire world to this secondary principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان الأصل الذي ذُمَّ به المجوس مما خالفوا به أهل الأديان من أوجه. ١ - أحدها أنهم قالوا كان الله واحداً لا شريك له. ثم حدثت منه فكرة رديئة، إما لما أصابته عينه، أو لما ظن أن يكون له عدو ينازمه. فإذا إبليس حدث من تلك الفكرة الرديئة؛ فخلق هو شر العالم، والله خيره، من غير أن كان الله قدرة على خلق شيء من الشر والفساد ونحو ذلك، أو لإبليس قدرة على خلق شيء من الخير والصلاح، فقام العالم بهما. وبهذا كله خالفوا أهل الأديان ومعلوم أن هذا كله أوصاف ذم ونعوت شين. ثم للمعتزلة عن كل هذه الصفات نصيب، فلذلك لقبوا باسم القدرية ولا قوة إلا بالله. ووجه ذلك أن المعتزلة زعمت أن الله تعالى كان ولا شيء غيره، ثم حدثت الإرادة من غير أن كان من الله بحدوثها إرادة، أو اختيار منه إليها. أفيكون لهذا القول معنى سوى أن كانت فكان بها جميع العالم؟ إذ من قولهم: إن العالم فعل الله، وإنه كان باختيار، وإن الاختيار إرادة، كقوله: فعّال لما يريد. قسمت المعتزلة تلك الحادثة إرادة والمجوس فكرة؛ وهي واحدة، بينهما اختلاف في الاسم لا في الحقيقة. ثم جعلت المجوس بها نصف العالم والمعتزلة كل العالم، فيكونان في الحاصل تحت قول ذميم، والمعتزلة زائدة.
And the basis for which the Magians were blamed, in that they opposed the people of religions, is from aspects. 1 - One of them is that they said: God was one, no partner with Him. Then there arose from Him a bad thought, either because his eye afflicted him, or because he thought that there would be an enemy contending with him. So Iblis arose from that bad thought; then he created the evil of the world, and God created its good, not that God had power to create anything of evil and corruption etc., nor that Iblis had power to create anything of good and righteousness, so the world was established by both. And by all this they opposed the people of religions, and it is known that all of this are attributes of blame and qualities of disgrace. Then the Mu'tazila have a share of all these attributes, therefore they were nicknamed with the name Qadariyya—and there is no power except with Allah. And the explanation of that is that the Mu'tazila claimed that God Almighty existed while nothing else existed, then will occurred without there being from God regarding its occurrence a will, or a choice from Him towards it. So does this statement have any meaning other than that it occurred and through it the whole world came to be? For from their statements is that the world is the act of God, and that it was by choice, and choice is will, like His saying: 'Doer of whatever He wills.' The Mu'tazila divided that occurring thing as will, and the Magians as thought; and it is one, between them is a difference in name not in reality. Then the Magians made half the world by it, and the Mu'tazila the whole world, so both are ultimately under a blameworthy saying, and the Mu'tazila more so.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect of blame against the Magians and shows how the Mu'tazila share in it. Maturidi explains that the Magians believed in an original monotheism but then introduced a 'bad thought' (فكرة رديئة) originating from God (out of envy or fear of an adversary), from which Iblis was generated. This Iblis then created all evil, while God created only good, setting up a dualistic universe. This belief contradicts all revealed religions. The author then states that the Mu'tazila have a share in these blameworthy attributes, hence they are called Qadariyya. The specific parallel is that the Mu'tazila claim that God existed alone, then 'will' occurred without any prior will or choice from God—i.e., an unoriginated will. Maturidi argues that this is essentially the same as the Magians' 'bad thought': both are a secondary principle from which the world originates. The only difference is that the Magians call it a 'thought' and assign only half the world (the evil half) to it, while the Mu'tazila call it 'will' and assign the entire world to it. Thus, the Mu'tazila are even more blameworthy because they attribute the whole creation to this secondary principle, effectively making God's will secondary to it.

إبليس
الإرادة الحادثة
الاختيار
الاسم
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are free from any form of dualism or associating partners with God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their view on the origin of will (as an unoriginated secondary principle) is essentially the same as the Magians' 'bad thought', making them even more extreme in attributing the whole world to this principle.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the core similarity between the Magians' 'thought' and the Mu'tazila's 'will'?
A) Both are eternal and uncreated
B) Both are a secondary principle that originates after God and from which the world comes to be
C) Both are directly created by God
D) Both are attributes of God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that both the Magians and the Mu'tazila introduce a secondary originated principle alongside God, differing only in name and scope, but both contradict tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a misfortune, do you ever feel that God was not fully in control of that event? In what subtle ways might you be attributing some power to 'chance' or 'nature'—isn't that the same dualism this lesson exposes? Will you repent and affirm that nothing occurs without His will alone?
?
You believe 'God wills everything', but have you ever reserved a corner of your heart for a secondary cause (like your own effort or someone else's decision) as if it operates independently? This lesson calls you to examine: is your tawhid pure, or do you unconsciously share lordship with a 'second principle'? Commit today to renew your shahada with this understanding.
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's justice, but fell into a dualism worse than the Magians. Are you willing to accept a theological position even if it seems logical but contradicts the pristine creed of the Salaf? Will you humbly submit to the revealed truth even when it challenges your reason?

The Mu'tazila-Magian Parallel on Creation
Maturidi enumerates parallels between Mu'tazila and Magians on created actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi parallels the Mu'tazila attribution of actions to creation with the Magian dualist framework.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila and Magians both deny God's direct creation of certain actions.

This passage is one of the most striking in Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid, where he draws a direct parallel between the Mu'tazila doctrine of human agency and the Zoroastrian dualism of the Magians. By comparing the two, Maturidi exposes what he sees as the logical consequence of denying God's exclusive creative power. Read carefully; the comparison is layered and precise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the parallel structure
Maturidi first describes the Mu'tazila position, then the Magian position, then shows how they mirror each other. Mark the specific claims attributed to each group.
Look for the phrases 'وكذلك' and 'وكذلك أمر المعتزلة' which signal the comparison.
3
Understand the symmetry of powers
Maturidi notes that Magians deny Iblis power over God's domain and God power over Iblis's domain, and that Mu'tazila do similarly but assign that power to living beings. Reflect on what this implies about their conception of divine sovereignty.
4
Ask: why does this comparison matter?
Consider why Maturidi would risk equating a Muslim sect with dualists. What doctrinal flaw is he exposing?
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid and divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - ثم المعتزلة تجعل العالم بالله وبالأجسام من غير أن كان ذلك من الله: من الاجتماع والتفرق، والحركة والسكون، وجميع المتولدات مما عن الخلق، مفصولاً [كان] أو بائنا. وكذلك جميع العالم عند المجوس من الخير والشر. بل المجوس ينسبون كثيرًا من الجواهر إلى إبليس [و]لا تقدر المعتزلة على نسبة شيء من ذلك إلى الله في الحقيقة. والمجوس يثبتون لإبليس القدرة على خلق الشر بالله وينفونها عن الله، وكذلك قول المعتزلة في قدرة أفعال الخلق. ولا قوة إلا بالله والمجوس لا تجعل لإبليس على شيء مما لله من العالم قدرة دون ولا الله على شيء مما هو لإبليس وكذلك أمر المعتزلة، لكنهم جعلوا الجميع للأحياء ذلك، والمجوس لإبليس خاصة. والمجوس لا تجعل الله إرادة ولا سلطانا فيما ليس فيه أمر، وكذلك المعتزلة والمعنى الذي دعا المجوس إلى القول باثنين ما استقبحوا من إضافة خلق الشر وفساد الأشياء إلى الله، وكذلك المعتزلة. ولو عرفوا [الربوبية] حق معرفة أنه في وضع كل شيء، موضعه، وأنه المتعالى عن أن يكون فعله لنفع له أو الخير يكتسب [ه] لنفسه لعلموا أن الوصف بخلق الكل على ما عليه وصف القدرة، والجلال والقول به قول بتمام الملك والكبرياء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
2 - Then the Mu'tazila make the world from God and from bodies, without that being from God: from composition and separation, and motion and rest, and all generated things from creation, whether separate or distant. And likewise all the world according to the Magians is from good and evil. Rather, the Magians attribute many substances to Iblis, and the Mu'tazila cannot attribute anything of that to God in reality. And the Magians affirm for Iblis the power to create evil with God and deny it from God, and likewise the saying of the Mu'tazila regarding the power of the actions of creation. And there is no power except with Allah. And the Magians do not make for Iblis over anything that is God's from the world any power without, nor God over anything that is Iblis's—and likewise the affair of the Mu'tazila, but they made all that for the living beings, while the Magians for Iblis specifically. And the Magians do not make for God will or authority in that which there is no command, and likewise the Mu'tazila. And the meaning that called the Magians to say two [principles] is that they deemed it repugnant to attribute creation of evil and corruption of things to God, and likewise the Mu'tazila. And if they had known [lordship] with true knowledge that He is in placing everything in its place, and that He is exalted above His act being for a benefit to Him or that He acquires good for Himself, they would know that describing [Him] with creating all as it is is a description of power and majesty, and saying it is a saying of complete dominion and grandeur. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage continues the comparison by presenting a second point of resemblance. Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila attribute the world to God and to bodies (i.e., the natural properties of bodies), but in a way that the motions, rest, compositions, separations, and all generated events are not from God directly but from the actions of created beings. This parallels the Magian view that the world's good and evil come from two separate sources: God and Iblis. The Magians attribute many substances to Iblis, while the Mu'tazila cannot attribute anything of that kind to God in reality—meaning they deny God's direct creation of evil and contingent events. Furthermore, the Magians affirm that Iblis has power to create evil 'with God' (i.e., by God's permission) and deny that God creates evil, which is exactly the Mu'tazila position regarding human actions: humans create their own acts (with power given by God) and God does not create them. Then Maturidi notes a symmetry: just as the Magians deny Iblis any power over what belongs to God and deny God power over what belongs to Iblis, the Mu'tazila hold a similar view but they assign all of that power to living beings (i.e., humans) rather than to Iblis specifically. Also, both groups deny God's will and authority over matters where there is no command (i.e., they limit God's will). The underlying motive for both is the same: they consider it repugnant to attribute the creation of evil and corruption to God. Maturidi concludes by reflecting that if they truly understood God's lordship—that He places everything in its proper place, and that His actions are not for His own benefit—they would realize that describing God as the creator of everything is a description of His power and majesty, and affirming it is to affirm His complete dominion and grandeur.

Key Terms
المتولدات
الاجتماع والتفرق
الحركة والسكون
القدرة
السلطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila position on human free will is distinct from Zoroastrian dualism.
Correction: Maturidi argues their logical structure is identical: both posit a source of action alongside God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the Mu'tazila attribute to bodies that they do not attribute directly to God?
A) The creation of substances
B) Composition, separation, motion, rest, and all generated things
C) The existence of good and evil
D) The power of will

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes that the Mu'tazila attribution of actions to creatures mirrors the Magian dualist structure, thus challenging the Islamic orthodoxy of their view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you instinctively blame your own 'wrong choice' as if it came entirely from you, forgetting that your very ability to choose is from Allah? How does Maturidi's exposure of the Mu'tazila-Magian parallel reveal the subtlety of this self-justification? What would it take for you to genuinely attribute every action, including your mistakes, to Allah's creation while still owning your responsibility?
?
The Mu'tazila gave humans a power that belongs only to Allah. In your daily life, where do you subtly claim sovereignty over your actions—in success, in decision-making—rather than acknowledging that every action is created by Allah? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself that 'there is no power except with Allah'?
?
Maturidi warns that attributing actions to ourselves can parallel the Magian dualism that puts a second source of creation alongside Allah. Do you ever think of your free will as an independent power that acts without Allah's direct involvement? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that Allah is the Creator of your actions, even your sins, without falling into fatalism?

The Common Motive and Its Correction
Maturidi identifies the common motive and offers a theological correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Mu'tazila and Magians share a common motive—repugnance to attribute evil to God—and how Maturidi corrects it by redefining true lordship.

Core Idea
Both Mu'tazila and Magians deny God's creation of evil due to misguided reverence.

After drawing the structural parallel, Maturidi now probes the psychological and theological motive behind both positions. He identifies a shared sense of repugnance at attributing evil to God, and then offers a profound corrective: true knowledge of lordship transforms how we view God's creation of all things. This is the heart of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the phrase 'ما استقبحوا من إضافة خلق الشر' — this is the core motive.
2
Trace the symmetry further
Maturidi mentions that both groups deny God's will and authority in certain matters. Note how he uses the phrase 'وكذلك المعتزلة' to show the parallel continues.
The denial of will (إرادة) and authority (سلطان) is a deeper point.
3
Grasp the corrective
Maturidi says 'if they had known lordship truly' they would see that describing God as creator of all is a description of power and majesty. Understand why this solves the problem.
This is the theological punchline: God's act is not for benefit; He is above that.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' — a reminder of absolute dependence on God. How does this reinforce the argument?

?

How does the Maturidi insight that God places everything in its proper place challenge your comfort zone? This week, catch yourself in a moment of discomfort and consciously say 'Allah created this' with full conviction.
?
The Mu'tazila and Magians avoided attributing evil to God out of a sense of reverence. Have you ever avoided acknowledging God's absolute control over a painful event because you felt it would be disrespectful or unfitting? How does the passage's teaching that affirming God's creation of all is an affirmation of His majesty change your perspective? Write down one situation you have been avoiding attributing to God, and say out loud: 'This too is from Allah, and He placed it perfectly.'
?
Maturidi says if they had true knowledge of lordship they would know God is exalted above acting for His own benefit. Do you sometimes judge God's actions or what happens to you based on what you perceive as beneficial or fair? This lesson calls you to submit to God's wisdom beyond your understanding. What is one area of your life where you need to stop measuring God's actions by your own standards and instead trust that He is placing everything in its proper place? Commit to reciting 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' every time you feel that tension.

The Divine Label of Qadariyya
Maturidi explains the divine origin of the Qadariyya label for the Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi claims the name Qadariyya is a divinely given label to identify the Mu'tazila as a blameworthy group.

Core Idea
The name Qadariyya is a divine sign, not a human insult.

In this brief but dense passage, Maturidi presents a second argument for why the Mu'tazila rightfully bear the name Qadariyya. He asserts that the name's universal usage among all people, regardless of their knowledge, proves it was ordained by Allah to mark a group worthy of avoidance. This highlights how theological labels can carry providential weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting that it begins with 'wa huna' and introduces another perspective. Pay attention to the key phrases: 'أنطق الله به ألسن الخلق' and 'بفضل الله'.
The passage is incomplete, so focus on the main point established.
2
Understand the argument
Maturidi says the name 'Qadariyya' is not a human invention but caused by God on all tongues. This universal usage shows it is a divine label.
Contrast this with the idea that the Mu'tazila chose the name themselves.
3
Identify the purpose
The label serves to identify 'ahl al-madhammah' (people of blame) so that others avoid mixing with them. Reflect on how this function strengthens the divine origin claim.
Maturidi then mentions two signs but the text cuts off; only one is partially given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و هنا عبارة أخرى: مما يبين أن المعتزلة أحق من يتعالى بالاسم من أهله ما أنطق الله به ألسن الخلق بالنسبة إليهم صغارهم وكبارهم من علم ما تحت الاسم أو جهله؛ فثبت أن ذلك صار لهم لقبا لا من حيث هو صنع للبشر فيه، ولكن بفضل الله، ليُعْلَم به أهل المذمة في الدين فيُحْذَر مخالطتهم. ولهم في ذلك علمان ظاهران. أحدهما في كون
And here is another expression: Among that which makes clear that the Mu'tazila are more worthy of being elevated by the name among its people is that which God caused the tongues of creation to utter regarding them, their young and their old, whether one knows what is under the name or is ignorant of it. So it is established that it became a nickname for them not by way of it being a making of humans therein, but by the favor of God, so that the people of blame in the religion may be known and their association may be avoided. And they have in that two apparent signs. One of them is in the being
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
In this concluding passage (which breaks off mid-sentence), Maturidi offers another perspective on why the Mu'tazila are rightfully called Qadariyya. He argues that the name was not coined by humans but was divinely ordained: God caused the tongues of people—both knowledgeable and ignorant, young and old—to apply this name to them. This widespread usage proves that it is a divinely given label (لقب) intended to identify the blameworthy group (أهل المذمة) so that others may avoid mixing with them. Maturidi then begins to mention two signs that demonstrate this, but only the first is partially given: 'one of them is in the being...' The passage is incomplete in the manuscript, so the full argument is not preserved here.

Key Terms

اللقب
فضل الله
أهل المذمة
علمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name 'Qadariyya' is simply a human-made insult used by opponents of the Mu'tazila.
Correction: Maturidi argues it is a divinely given nickname (لقب) by God's favor, recognized by all people, to identify a blameworthy faction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what indicates that the name 'Qadariyya' is a divinely given label rather than a human invention?
A) The Mu'tazila themselves chose it
B) God caused all people, young and old, to utter it against them
C) The Prophet explicitly said it in the hadith
D) The scholars agreed to use it

Key Takeaway
Maturidi grounds the Qadariyya label in divine action, making it a theological marker rather than a human epithet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the term 'Qadariyya' or other sectarian labels, do you immediately dismiss them as mere human insults? How does Maturidi's claim that such labels have divine origin challenge you to reconsider your attitude towards those groups?
?
If God has placed clear markers to warn believers against harmful groups, are you paying attention to those markers, or are you ignoring them as outdated? What specific step will you take this week to guard your faith against influences that contradict sound 'aqeedah?
?
Maturidi says the purpose of the label is to warn believers so they avoid mixing with ahl al-dhimma. In your daily life, have you been complacent about whose company you keep? Is there a person or group you need to distance yourself from out of obedience to this divine warning?

The Mu'tazila-Majus Parallel on Evil and Power
Maturidi enumerates parallels between Mu'tazila and Majus on evil and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific parallels Maturidi draws between the Mu'tazila and the Majus regarding the source of evil and divine power.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila and Majus both shield God from evil by attributing it to another creator.

Maturidi begins a sharp polemic by comparing the Mu'tazila to the Zoroastrians (Majus). Both groups, he argues, find it repugnant to ascribe evil to God and therefore posit another agent—Iblis or human beings—as the creator of evil. This lesson explores the specific parallels and the underlying theological motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the comparison passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the repeated structure 'كذلك المعتزلة' (likewise the Mu'tazila).
This passage is highly polemical; focus on the doctrinal claims.
2
Identify the parallels
List each point where Maturidi equates the Mu'tazila stance with that of the Majus. There are at least four specific parallels.
Look for statements about power, will, and ascription of evil.
3
Understand the dualism motive
Maturidi says the Majus were led to dualism because they found it repugnant to ascribe evil to God. He claims the Mu'tazila share this motive.
This is the core theological criticism.
4
Reflect on the alternative
Consider Maturidi's corrective: true lordship means God places everything in its proper place, and creating all things is a description of power and majesty.
This connects to the lesson's objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بائنا . وكذلك جميع العالم عند المجوس من الخير والشر. بل المجوس ينسبون كثيرًا من الجواهر إلى إبليس ولا تقدر المعتزلة على نسبة شيء من ذلك إلى الله في الحقيقة. والمجوس يثبتون لإبليس القدرة على خلق الشر بالله وينفونها عن الله ، وكذلك قول المعتزلة في قدرة أفعال الخلق . ولا قوة إلا بالله والمجوس لا تجعل لإبليس على شيء - مما الله ـ من العالم قدرة دولار ولا الله لا الله على شيء مما هو لإبليس وكذلك أمر المعتزلة، لكنهم جعلوا الجميع الأحياء ذلك، والمجوس لإبليس خاصة . والمجوس لا تجعل الله إرادة ولا سلطانا فيما ليس فيه أمر ، وكذلك المعتزلة والمعنى الذي دعا المجوس إلى القول باثنين ما استقبحوا من إضافة خلق الشر وفساد الأشياء إلى الله، وكذلك المعتزلة. ولو عرفوا الربوبية حق معرفة أنه في وضع كل شيء موضعه، وأنه المتعالى عن أن يكون فعله لنفع له أو الخير يكتسبه لنفسه لعلموا أن الوصف بخلق الكل على ما عليه وصف القدرة ، والجلال والقول به قول بتمام الملك والكبرياء. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
ـ و هنا عبارة أخرى : مما يبين أن المعتزلة أحق من يتعالى بالاسم من أهله ما أنطق الله به ألسن الخلق بالنسبة إليهم صغارهم وكبارهم مَن علم ما تحت الاسم أو جهله ؛
فثبت أن ذلك صار لهم لقبا لا من حيث هو صنع للبشر فيه، ولكن بفضل الله، ليُعلم به أهل المذمة في الدين فيُحذر مخالطتهم . ولهم في ذلك علمان ظاهران. أحدهما في كون كل منهم على حسن خلقته وقبحها أن تظهر في وجه كل منهم الصفرة الباردة التي تستقبحها الأبصار، إذا قوبل ذلك بوجوه المجوس لوجدوا سواء. والثاني تخلفهم على حانات المجوس وإنكار عامتهم دار الإسلام من أن تكون دارهم ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولتحقيق هذا الاسم لهم أيضاً وجهان. أحدهما أن كل ذي دين ومذهب نسب إلى المعنى الذي ادعاه لنفسه بحق الإسلام واليهودية والنصرانية ونحو هذا. وكذلك المعتزلة، يرون قدر أفعالهم لأنفسهم وغيرهم يرون ذلك منه . فمحال أن يشتهر به من رآه لغيره، ويُزال عمن يدعى حقيقته لنفسه. وبمثله جاء عن رسول الله له في شرط الإيمان والإيمان بالقدر خيره وشره من الله . والوجه الآخر هو الأمر المعروف الذي لم نر معتزليا سلم عما يزيل عنه اسم الإيمان وتحلى بحلية الإسلام من ارتكاب الكبائر بالشهوات مما يبين استخفافهم بدين الله واختيارهم الخروج منه بأدنى شهوة أعطوها لأنفسهم. فهم أحق من يُنسب إلى غير دين الله ، إذ هذا شأنهم في دينهم الذي هو عندهم دين الله . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Separated? Likewise, all the world according to the Magians is from good and evil. Rather, the Magians ascribe many essences/causes to Iblis, and the Mu'tazila cannot ascribe any of that to God in reality. The Magians affirm for Iblis the ability to create evil by God, and they deny it of God; likewise the saying of the Mu'tazila concerning the power of the acts of creatures. There is no power but in God. The Magians do not assign to Iblis any power over anything of the world that belongs to God, and God has no power over anything that belongs to Iblis; likewise the matter of the Mu'tazila, except that they (the Mu'tazila) made all living things that, while the Magians made it specific to Iblis. The Magians do not attribute to God will or authority in that which He has not commanded; likewise the Mu'tazila. The meaning that led the Magians to dualism is that they considered it repugnant to ascribe the creation of evil and corruption of things to God; likewise the Mu'tazila. Had they known lordship truly—that He places everything in its proper place, and that He is exalted above His act being for His own benefit or acquiring good for Himself—they would have known that the description of creating all as it is a description of power and majesty, and affirming it is affirming perfect sovereignty and grandeur. There is no power but in God.
— And here is another expression: Among what shows that the Mu'tazila are more deserving of being distinguished by the name (Qadariyya) from its proper people is what God has caused the tongues of creatures to speak concerning them—their young and old, whether one knows what is under the name or is ignorant of it.
So it is established that this became a nickname for them, not because it is a human invention, but by God's grace, so that the people of blame in religion might be known and mixing with them be avoided. They have two apparent signs in that. One of them is that each of them, whether their constitution is good or ugly, a cold pallor appears on their face which eyes consider ugly; when compared to the faces of the Magians, they are found to be the same. The second is their frequenting the taverns of the Magians and the denial of the generality of them that the Abode of Islam is their abode. There is no power but in God.
To confirm this name for them, there are also two aspects. The first: every adherent of a religion or sect is ascribed to the meaning he claims for himself by right—Islam, Judaism, Christianity, and the like. Likewise the Mu'tazila: they see the power of their own acts for themselves, and they see that same power for others? So it is impossible that one who sees it for another should be famous for it, and that he who claims its reality for himself should be stripped of it. And similar to this has come from the Messenger of God ﷺ concerning the condition of faith: faith in destiny, its good and evil, is from God. The other aspect is the well-known matter: we have never seen a Mu'tazilite who is safe from that which removes the name of faith from him and who is adorned with the ornament of Islam, after committing major sins through desires, showing their contempt for God's religion and their choosing to exit from it for the slightest desire they have given to themselves. So they are more deserving of being ascribed to a religion other than God's, since this is their conduct concerning their religion which they consider to be God's religion. There is no power but in God.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a polemical comparison by Imam al-Maturidi between the Mu'tazila and the Magians (Zoroastrians), followed by a proof that the Mu'tazila are the true 'Qadariyya' (those who deny divine predestination). The author begins by stating that the Magians ascribe many essences to Iblis, but the Mu'tazila cannot ascribe any of that to God in reality—they deny God's power over evil. The Magians affirm Iblis's power to create evil with God's permission and deny that power to God, and similarly the Mu'tazila affirm a power for human acts. The Magians do not assign Iblis power over anything that belongs to God, and God has no power over what belongs to Iblis; the Mu'tazila are similar except that they make all living beings have that power, while the Magians restrict it to Iblis. The Magians do not attribute will or authority to God in matters He has not commanded; likewise the Mu'tazila. The underlying reason for the Magians' dualism is their unwillingness to ascribe evil's creation to God; the Mu'tazila share this motive. If they understood lordship correctly—that God places everything in its proper place, and He is exalted beyond acting for His own benefit—they would realize that describing God as Creator of all is a description of power and majesty, and affirming it affirms perfect sovereignty. The author then introduces another expression showing that the Mu'tazila are more deserving of the name Qadariyya (here, a blameworthy nickname) because God has caused the tongues of creatures to call them by it. He says this nickname became a label, not by human invention, but by God's grace to warn people against them. They have two visible signs: (1) their faces show a cold pallor, and when compared to Magian faces they are identical; (2) they frequent Magian taverns and deny that the Abode of Islam is their abode. To confirm the name, two rational aspects are given: (a) Every religious group is named after what they claim; the Mu'tazila claim power over their own acts and see it in themselves, yet they deny it to God—so they are rightly called Qadariyya. (b) No Mu'tazilite is free from major sins that remove the name of faith, showing their contempt for religion; they are therefore more deserving of being ascribed to a religion other than God's. The passage concludes with 'There is no power but in God.'

Key Terms
المجوس
المعتزلة
إبليس
القدرة
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila are identical to the Majus in all beliefs.
Correction: Maturidi only highlights specific parallels in their view of evil and power, not a total equivalence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the main reason the Majus and Mu'tazila share a similar view on evil?
A) Both reject the existence of God.
B) Both find it repugnant to ascribe evil to God.
C) Both believe in two eternal principles.
D) Both deny free will.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for sharing with the Majus the fundamental error of shielding God from evil by positing another creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience or witness evil, do you ever feel a sense of repugnance that leads you to excuse God from it, even in your heart? In what ways might you subtly attribute power to others (like people or forces) rather than submitting to God's absolute sovereignty over all events?
?
Maturidi says that if they knew lordship truly, they would know that describing God as Creator of all is a description of power and majesty. Do you truly believe that every single event, including those you find painful or unjust, is from God? If not, what within you resists that belief, and how might you cultivate that trust?
?
The Mu'tazila and Magians both considered it repugnant to ascribe evil to God. Have you ever felt that repugnance? How do you reconcile the reality of evil with God's perfect goodness? Does this lesson challenge you to accept that God's placing of all things includes what you perceive as evil?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
10 min
Proof that the Mu'tazila are the True Qadariyya
Maturidi presents divine, visible, and rational proofs that Mu'tazila are the Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two signs and two rational proofs Maturidi uses to show that the Mu'tazila are more deserving of the blameworthy name 'Qadariyya' than the original group.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are the true Qadariyya by divine decree, visible signs, and their own doctrine and conduct.

In the second part of the passage, Maturidi shifts from comparison to proof. He argues that the Mu'tazila are rightfully called 'Qadariyya'—a blameworthy label—because God caused people's tongues to name them so, and because of objective signs and rational considerations. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof section
Read from 'مما يبين أن المعتزلة أحق...' to the end. Notice the structure: divine sign, two visible signs, two rational aspects.
The passage is dense; use the translation alongside.
2
Understand the divine sign
Maturidi claims that God has caused all people—knowingly or ignorantly—to call the Mu'tazila 'Qadariyya'. This is a divine indication.
This is a theological argument from common usage.
3
Examine the two visible signs
The first sign is a physical resemblance: Mu'tazila have a pale complexion like Majus. The second is behavioral: they frequent Majus taverns and reject the Abode of Islam.
These signs are meant to be observable.
4
Analyze the two rational aspects
The first rational aspect: every group is named after what they claim; Mu'tazila claim power over their own acts, so they are Qadariyya. The second: their sinful conduct removes them from the name of faith, making them deserving of a non-Islamic label.
These are logical arguments based on naming conventions and behavior.

Commentary
This passage is a polemical comparison by Imam al-Maturidi between the Mu'tazila and the Magians (Zoroastrians), followed by a proof that the Mu'tazila are the true 'Qadariyya' (those who deny divine predestination). The author begins by stating that the Magians ascribe many essences to Iblis, but the Mu'tazila cannot ascribe any of that to God in reality—they deny God's power over evil. The Magians affirm Iblis's power to create evil with God's permission and deny that power to God, and similarly the Mu'tazila affirm a power for human acts. The Magians do not assign Iblis power over anything that belongs to God, and God has no power over what belongs to Iblis; the Mu'tazila are similar except that they make all living beings have that power, while the Magians restrict it to Iblis. The Magians do not attribute will or authority to God in matters He has not commanded; likewise the Mu'tazila. The underlying reason for the Magians' dualism is their unwillingness to ascribe evil's creation to God; the Mu'tazila share this motive. If they understood lordship correctly—that God places everything in its proper place, and He is exalted beyond acting for His own benefit—they would realize that describing God as Creator of all is a description of power and majesty, and affirming it affirms perfect sovereignty. The author then introduces another expression showing that the Mu'tazila are more deserving of the name Qadariyya (here, a blameworthy nickname) because God has caused the tongues of creatures to call them by it. He says this nickname became a label, not by human invention, but by God's grace to warn people against them. They have two visible signs: (1) their faces show a cold pallor, and when compared to Magian faces they are identical; (2) they frequent Magian taverns and deny that the Abode of Islam is their abode. To confirm the name, two rational aspects are given: (a) Every religious group is named after what they claim; the Mu'tazila claim power over their own acts and see it in themselves, yet they deny it to God—so they are rightly called Qadariyya. (b) No Mu'tazilite is free from major sins that remove the name of faith, showing their contempt for religion; they are therefore more deserving of being ascribed to a religion other than God's. The passage concludes with 'There is no power but in God.'

According to the passage, what is the first rational aspect that proves the Mu'tazila deserve the name Qadariyya?
A) They have pale faces like the Majus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your daily actions, do you ever catch yourself attributing your successes to your own power rather than to God? How does Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazila 'see the power of their own acts' challenge you this week?
?
The passage says the Mu'tazila have 'two visible signs' that reveal their true state. If others looked at your conduct, what visible signs would they see that confirm your faith? Are there any signs that might suggest otherwise? What will you change today to align your outward signs with inward belief?
?
Maturidi uses the term 'people of blame in religion' for those who deserve the label Qadariyya. Are there any beliefs or habits in your life that, if known, would place you among 'the people of blame'? What is one step you can take tonight to repent and seek refuge in God's power alone?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate

Maturidi's Refutation of the Pre-Islamic Origin of 'Qadariyya'
Maturidi refutes the claim that Arabs used Qadariyya before Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the term 'Qadariyya' was not a pre-Islamic Arab nickname but rather a later designation based on prophetic condemnation.

Core Idea
The name 'Qadariyya' came from prophetic condemnation, not Arab custom.

Maturidi addresses a claim by al-Ka'bi that the Arabs used to call people Qadariyya based on frequent speech. He argues that such naming was only among common people, not the elite, and that the Prophet's condemnation of Qadariyya came before any sect existed, so it cannot refer to the Mu'tazila. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim being refuted
Before reading Maturidi's argument, understand that he is responding to someone (likely al-Ka'bi) who claimed the Arabs used the term Qadariyya as a nickname for those who frequently spoke about destiny.
This context helps you see why Maturidi focuses on social distinction.
2
Examine Maturidi's social distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between the common people (awamm) and the elite (khawass). He argues that only the common people used such nicknames; the elite disliked them. Therefore, any alleged pre-Islamic usage was not authoritative.
Notice the phrase 'إنما يذكره العوام' (only the common people mention it).
3
Understand the chronological argument
Maturidi points out that the Prophet's condemnation of 'Qadariyya' came before any group was known by that belief or had a sect. Thus, the name cannot apply to the Mu'tazila, who emerged later.
The repetition of 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' emphasizes reliance on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرب . والثاني ما حكى عنهم هم لا يقولون ذلك، وإن كان يقوله فلا يقوله من بهم يُعرف أسماء النحل ؛ إنما يذكره العوام، فأما الخواص فهم لا يذكرون ذلك، بل يكرهون ذكر ذلك خشية أن يذكر على الاعتذار فيما لا عذر لهم والعرب لو عملت الذي قال إنما عملت فيمن ظهر على التلقيب لا للتحقيق؛ ونحن فيما حقه التحقيق لما عن رسول الله جاء ذلك فدم أهله. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضاً إن الذم جاء من عند رسول الله ، ولم يكن في ذلك الوقت من يُعرف بهذا الفعل، ولا كانت النحلة التي أبدعت العرب لها الاسم، فلا يحتمل الاسم الذي قال لهذا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Arabs. And the second thing that was narrated about them is that they do not say that. And if one of them says it, he is not one by whom the names of sects are known; only the common people mention it. As for the elite, they do not mention that; rather, they dislike mentioning it, fearing that it might be mentioned as an excuse in matters where they have no excuse. And if the Arabs did what he said, they did it only for one who appears to be nicknamed, not for truth. And we, in what deserves truth, when that came from the Messenger of God, it came to its people. And there is no power except with God. Also, the condemnation came from the Messenger of God, and at that time there was no one known for this act, nor was there the sect for which the Arabs invented the name. So the name he said does not apply to this. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument made by al-Ka'bi that the Arabs used to call people by a name based on their frequent speech. Maturidi argues that the Arabs, in their naming of sects, only used terms among the common people, not the elite. The elite disliked using such names as excuses. Even if the Arabs did use nicknames, they applied them to those who outwardly deserved them, not for true attribution. Maturidi insists that the term 'Qadariyya' came from the Prophet's condemnation, and at that time there was no one known for that belief, nor was there a sect that the Arabs had named. Therefore, the name cannot apply to the Mu'tazila as they claim. He repeats 'There is no power except with God' to affirm reliance on God.

Key Terms
القدرية
النحلة
الخواص
العوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name 'Qadariyya' was used by the Arabs before Islam as a general term for those who deny fate.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the term was only used by common people, not elites, and that the Prophet's condemnation preceded the existence of any sect, so the name is not a pre-Islamic Arab invention.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, who among the Arabs used terms like 'Qadariyya'?
A) The elite (khawass)
B) The common people (awamm)
C) Both equally
D) No one used it

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the claim that 'Qadariyya' was a pre-Islamic Arab nickname by showing it was only used by common people and that the Prophet's condemnation predates the existence of any such sect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear or use sectarian labels like 'Qadari' or 'Jahmi', do you consider whether they truly apply, or do you follow the habit of the common people? How can Maturidi's caution lead you to stop and verify before labeling?
?
Think of a time you condemned someone based on a label without knowing the full history or their actual belief. How does Maturidi's argument that the Prophet's condemnation came before any sect existed challenge you to separate historical facts from personal judgments?
?
Maturidi says the elite dislike even mentioning such names. Is there a sectarian label you feel comfortable using? What would it take for you to join the elite in avoiding such terms out of fear of injustice?

Maturidi's Responses to Objections on Qadariyya Naming
Maturidi answers two objections about calling Mu'tazila Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila are rightly called Qadariyya based on their affirmation of independent human power, and why they do not become Qadariyya merely by using the word 'taqdir'.

Core Idea
Naming follows affirmation, not negation.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses two objections from a questioner who is confused about the application of the term 'Qadariyya' to the Mu'tazila. The first objection concerns the ascription of 'no power' to God; the second concerns the Mu'tazila's own statement 'we estimate our deeds'. Maturidi's replies clarify the principles of theological naming and the specific meaning of Qadariyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist of the two objections and Maturidi's responses.
Notice the two distinct objections.
2
Analyze the first objection
Understand why the one who negates (النافي) is not named after what he negates. Maturidi says the name is given to the claimant and affirmer (المدعي والمثبت).
This establishes a key principle: naming follows affirmation.
3
Analyze the second objection
Consider why using the verb 'qaddara' (to estimate) does not automatically make one Qadariyya. Maturidi gives two reasons: the name derives from 'muqaddir' (one who estimates) and such estimation is common to all people.
The second reason shows that the term is not applied to everyday estimation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل عنا سؤالاً دل على حيرته فقال: «نسبتم إليه بقولكم : لا قدر» فأجاب بأن لا ينسب الشيء إلى النافي. {قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } وما قاله صدق، وإنما ينسب إلى المدعي والمثبت لنفسه، وهو حيث يقول : تخرج الأفعال على قدرة / التي قدر [العبد] عليها . ثم قال : لو قيل : أثبتم ذلك بقولكم : «نحن نقدر أعمالنا». قال: لا يجب لوجهين. أحدهما أن الاسم منه مُقدِّر . والثاني أنه لا تمانع له في القول : إنه يقدّر صلاته وثوبه وداره وأمر سفره، فيجب أن يكونوا كلهم قدرية.
Then he asked us a question indicating his confusion, saying: 'You ascribe to Him by your saying: "No power."' So he answered that a thing is not ascribed to the one who negates. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: What he said is true. Rather, it is ascribed to the one who claims and affirms it for himself, when he says: Actions come forth by a power that the slave has been given power over. Then he said: If it is said: You have affirmed that by your saying: 'We have power over (nuqaddir) our deeds.' He said: It is not necessary for two reasons. One is that the name [Qadariyya] comes from 'muqaddir' (one who estimates). The second is that there is no prohibition for him in saying: He estimates his prayer, his garment, his house, and the matter of his journey. So they would all be Qadariyya.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a response to two objections from a questioner. The first objection: you (the Mu'tazila) ascribe to God that He has no power by saying 'no power' (la qadr). Maturidi replies that a person is not named by what he denies, but by what he affirms. The Mu'tazila affirm power for themselves, so they are called Qadariyya because they attribute power to themselves rather than to God. The second objection: if you affirm that you have power over your deeds (nuqaddir a'malana), does that make you Qadariyya? Maturidi answers that it is not necessary for two reasons. First, the name 'Qadariyya' derives from 'muqaddir' meaning one who estimates or determines, which can refer to everyday estimation. Second, if one can say 'I estimate my prayer, my garment, etc.' then that would make everyone Qadariyya, which is absurd. The term only applies to the specific belief about predestination.

Key Terms
النافي
المدعي
المثبت
قدرة
مقدّر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being called Qadariyya is based on any use of the word 'qadr' or 'taqdir'.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between theological claim of independent power and mundane estimation. Only the former makes one Qadariyya.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that ascribing 'no power' to God does not make one Qadariyya?
A) Because the Mu'tazila affirm power for themselves, not deny it for God
B) Because naming is based on what one denies, not what one affirms
C) Because the term Qadariyya only applies to those who deny God's power
D) Because the questioner is confused

Key Takeaway
The name Qadariyya is rightly applied to those who claim independent power for themselves, not to those who deny God's power or use estimation in mundane matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly attribute all your successes to God's power, or do you secretly believe your own ability earned them? Will you examine your heart right now and repent for any hidden reliance on yourself?
?
When you plan your day—your prayer, your work, your travel—do you say 'I will do this' as if you have independent control? How much do you truly depend on God's decree? Can you commit to saying 'inshaAllah' with full meaning?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed power for themselves. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are the sole decider, forgetting that God is the ultimate disposer? Will you consciously surrender that domain to Him today?

Maturidi's Analysis of the Name Qadariyya
Maturidi traces the etymology of Qadariyya and argues it refers to a specific belief about decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi derives the name Qadariyya from the root 'qadr' and why it applies specifically to the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The name Qadariyya is not about God's power but about a human belief that actions come from their own decree.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi examines the linguistic and theological basis for the term 'Qadariyya'. He contrasts it with terms like 'Nasrani' and 'Yahudi', showing that the name stems from a specific doctrine about decree. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi uses analogy and conditional reasoning.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to 'فأما' and 'والثاني'.
2
Understand the analogy with Christian/Jew
Maturidi compares the derivation of 'Qadari' from 'qadr' to 'Nasrani' from 'tanaṣṣara' and 'Yahudi' from 'tahawwada'. What does this analogy imply about the basis of the name?
The action of 'becoming a follower' gives the name.
3
Grasp why Mu'tazila are called Qadariyya
After excluding God from the name (since He is 'Qadir' not 'Qadari'), Maturidi concludes the name refers either to a specific religious doctrine or a meaning in the person. He then states that the Mu'tazila claim actions go out by their decree, so they deserve the name.
This is the key doctrinal point: Qadariyya are those who attribute actions to their own qadr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فأما الحرف الأول فقدر وقدر واحد. وبعد، فإن الفعل في النصراني واليهودى التنصر والتهود، والاسم على ما يرى فمثله في القدر. والثاني قد يُسمى الله تعالى بذلك، ثم لا يقال «قدرى». فثبت أن ذلك يرجع إلى أمر خاص، أو إلى معنى فيمن إليه . فإن كان إلى أمر خاص فهو في الدين، ومن نسبه إلى نفسه فهو أحق به، وإن كان المرجع فيه إلى المعنى فهو لأنهم على ذلك القول يرون حقيقة الخروج على قدر الله لا على قدر العبد . والمعتزلة تزعم أنه على قدرهم يخرج. والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: As for the first letter, it is 'qadr' and 'qadr' are one. And moreover, the action in the Christian and the Jew is 'becoming Christian' and 'becoming Jewish', and the name is as you see; similarly in 'qadr'. The second [point] is that God the Exalted may be named by that [i.e., Qadir], yet He is not called 'Qadari'. So it is established that this [name] refers to something specific, or to a meaning in the one to whom it is attributed. If it refers to something specific, then it is in religion, and whoever attributes it to himself is more deserving of it. And if the reference is to the meaning, then it is because they, according to that saying, see the reality of going out against God's decree, not against the slave's decree. And the Mu'tazila claim that it goes out by their decree. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is an analysis by Maturidi of the term 'Qadariyya'. He explains that the word 'qadr' has a single root. Just as the actions of becoming Christian or Jewish derive names from the religion, so does 'qadr'. However, God is named 'Qadir' (Powerful) but not 'Qadari', indicating the name is not a general attribute. Therefore, the name 'Qadariyya' refers either to a specific religious matter (i.e., belief about predestination) or to a meaning in the person named. If it is a specific religious matter, then whoever attributes it to himself deserves the name. If it refers to the meaning, it is because they believe that actions come forth by their own decree (qadr) rather than by God's decree. The Mu'tazila claim that actions occur by their own power, hence they are rightfully called Qadariyya.

Key Terms
قدر
التنصر
التهود
قدرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the name 'Qadari' is used for anyone who discusses qadr or for God.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between 'Qadir' for God and 'Qadari' for human sects. 'Qadariyya' refers to a specific human doctrine that attributes actions to one's own decree, not to God's power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is God not called 'Qadari' even though He is named 'Qadir'?
A) Because 'Qadari' implies a specific human doctrine about decree
B) Because Arabic grammar does not allow it
C) Because the Qur'an prohibits it
D) Because God's power is different from human power

Key Takeaway
The name Qadariyya is a specific label for those who attribute the origin of actions to their own decree, not a general term for discussing divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something, do you truly feel it is by God's decree, or do you silently credit your own will? In what subtle ways does your heart claim a 'Qadari' independence?
?
Maturidi says the Qadariyya 'see the reality of going out against God's decree' by asserting their own. Ask yourself: is there an area of life where you resist submission to God's plan, insisting on your own way? Will you surrender that today?
?
If someone were to accuse you of being a Qadari, would your actions show that you rely on your own decree or on God's? What one change can you make this week to live more fully under God's qadr?

Maturidi on Naming and the Mu'tazila
Maturidi refutes a naming argument by counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rebuttal to the argument that sects are named by frequent speech, using the Mu'tazila as an example.

Core Idea
Naming by doctrine, not by frequency of speech.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a claim that sects are named after the terms they frequently use. He turns the argument on its head, showing that if that were true, the Mu'tazila would be called Jabariyya. He then clarifies that true naming is based on the reality of the doctrine, not on mere verbal habits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp Maturidi's concise style.
Notice the repetition of 'There is no power except with God.'
2
Identify the two groups
Find the Mu'tazila and the Magians in the passage. Note what Maturidi says about each with respect to naming.
The Mu'tazila are accused of frequent 'jabr', the Magians are named by doctrine.
3
Understand the logical twist
Maturidi uses the opponent's own criterion (frequency) against them: if frequency determines naming, the Mu'tazila deserve the name Jabariyya. Then he rejects that criterion entirely by citing the Magians.
This is a classic polemical move.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why naming by reality of doctrine is important. It prevents superficial labeling and emphasizes theological accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قال من العرب فيجب أن يكون المعتزلة لهم اسم الجبرية لكثرة ما يجري على لسانهم اسم الجبر . ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما نسب إلى المجوس ، وهم لا بكثرة القول سموا به، ولكن بحقيقة المذهب. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And what he said about the Arabs: then the Mu'tazila must have the name 'Jabariyya' because of the frequency with which the word 'jabr' runs on their tongues. And there is no power except with God. Along with what was attributed to the Magians, and they were not named by the frequency of speech, but by the reality of the school. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of the argument that the Arabs named groups by frequency. Maturidi says that if that were the case, the Mu'tazila themselves would deserve the name 'Jabariyya' because they frequently use the word 'jabr' (compulsion). He then mentions the Magians (Zoroastrians) as an example: they were not named by frequency but by the actual doctrine they hold. Thus, naming should be based on true belief, not on frequent speech. He concludes with 'There is no power except with God' to emphasize divine support.

Key Terms
الجبرية
المجوس
حقيقة المذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sects are named after the terms they frequently use.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila frequently use 'jabr,' so they would be called Jabariyya by that logic, but in reality they are not. The Magians are named by their actual doctrine, not by frequent words.

Checkpoint

A) The Magians (مجوس)
B) The Christians
C) The Jews
D) The Polytheists

Key Takeaway
Names of theological groups reflect their core doctrines, not the words they commonly utter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Maturidi uses the example of the Magians, whose name reflects their core doctrine. What is your 'core doctrine' as reflected in your actions? If others named your faith by your daily choices rather than your declarations, what would they call you? Are you satisfied with that name, and what will you change today to align your reality with your claim of Iman?

The True Origin of the Term Qadariyya
Maturidi responds to the claim that the Hashwiyya invented the name Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the name 'Qadariyya' for the Mu'tazila is not an invention of the Hashwiyya but originates from a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The term Qadariyya comes from the Prophet, not from sectarian rivalry.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to an accusation that the Hashwiyya (traditionalist scholars) coined the term 'Qadariyya' for the Mu'tazila as a slur. He dismisses the claim as false and traces the name back to the Prophet's own words. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the abrupt ending—the hadith is cut off.
The passage contains brackets indicating unclear or missing text.
2
Identify the two claims
First, the opponent claims the Hashwiyya invented the name due to their errors and alliance with Banu Umayyad. Second, Maturidi refutes this and provides the true origin.
3
Understand the role of Banu Umayyad
The opponent says the Hashwiyya joined the Umayyads to absolve them of sin by attributing evil acts to God's decree. Maturidi says this is mostly false.
4
Grasp Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi states that the name is inherited from the entire community via a prophetic hadith: 'Two groups...' (cut off). The Hashwiyya did not invent it.
5
Reflect on the significance
This shows that even sectarian labels have prophetic roots, and accusations of innovation can be unfounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن وجه تسمية الحشوية لهم قدرية، فزعم أن ذا من خطئهم نحو خطئهم في أكثر أمور الدين مع ما انضموا إلى بني مروان، وذلك كان مذهبهم، ليفرحوا بإضافتهم الأفعال الذميمة إلى قضاء الله وقدره. فساعدوهم على ذلك، وبرؤهم عن الذنب بما اقترفوا في الحمل على الله . ورأوا ذلك شائعا لهم ، كفعل " معاوية بعمار " ، إنه " قتله، [ وقال: «قتله] على حيث جاء به ، وقولهم : الذي تولى كبره علي وعظم قول المعتزلة فيهم حيث أخرجوهم " وأطنب في هذا [ب] الذي أكثره كذب . {قال الفقيه : } أما نسبة التسمية إلى الحشوية / فإنما هو تمويههم ليروا أن الذي سماهم [١٦٦] بهذا هم، وإنما هذه النسبة " " متوارثة في الأمة بأسرها، في خبر عن النبي عليه السلام: «صنفان
Then he was asked about the reason for the Hashwiyya naming them Qadariyya. So he claimed that this was from their error, like their error in most matters of religion, along with their joining Banu Umayyad, and that was their school, in order to rejoice by attributing blameworthy actions to God's decree and predestination. So they helped them in that, and absolved them of sin by what they had perpetrated in burdening God. And they saw that as common among them, like the act of Mu'awiya with 'Ammar: indeed, he killed him, and [said: 'He killed him] on the basis of what he brought, and their saying: 'The one who took upon himself the great part of it is 'Ali' and they magnified the saying of the Mu'tazila regarding them when they expelled them.' And he exaggerated in this [b] most of which is false. The Faqih said: As for the attribution of the name to the Hashwiyya, it is only their deception to make it seem that the one who named them with this is them, but this attribution is inherited in the entire community, in a report from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Two groups...'
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: why do the Hashwiyya (a term for Sunni hadith folk) call the Mu'tazila Qadariyya? The questioner claims it is due to their errors and collaboration with the Umayyads to absolve them of sins. Maturidi dismisses this as mostly false. Then he gives the true reason: the name Qadariyya is not an invention of the Hashwiyya but is transmitted from the Prophet himself, as a well-known hadith states: 'Two groups are the Qadariyya...' (the hadith is cut off). The Hashwiyya are only using the term that the entire community has inherited from the Prophet. So the accusation that they invented it is deceptive.

Key Terms
الحشوية
بني مروان
قضاء الله
قدره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term Qadariyya was invented by the Hashwiyya as a derogatory label for the Mu'tazila.
Correction: Maturidi states the term originates from the Prophet's own words, passed down through the entire community, not invented by the Hashwiyya.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, where does the name 'Qadariyya' truly originate?
A) From the Hashwiyya
B) From Banu Umayyad
C) From the Prophet's hadith
D) From the Mu'tazila themselves

Key Takeaway
The name Qadariyya for the Mu'tazila is rooted in a prophetic hadith, not in sectarian invention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does the prophetic origin challenge you to treat those so-named with more fairness? What would it take for you to truly follow the Prophet's designation rather than the sectarian narratives?
