Naming of Sects: Jabriyya and Qadariyya in Light of Hadith
Maturidi discusses sect naming and cites a hadith on Qadariyya and Murji'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the hadith about Qadariyya and Murji'a to clarify the proper naming of sects and refute polemical attributions.

Core Idea
Sect names are rooted in doctrine, not in frequency of speech.

This passage addresses a controversial topic in kalam: how do we name theological groups? Maturidi argues that sectarian labels must reflect real doctrinal positions, not rhetorical habits. He then grounds the name 'Qadariyya' in a prophetic hadith, showing that the term is inherited from the early community, not invented by opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the shift from the initial claim about the Mu'tazila to the final hadith citation.
The passage contains a mix of argument and historical accusation.
2
Identify the key sect names
Note the terms: Jabriyya (for Mu'tazila), Qadariyya, Murji'a, and Hashwiyya. Understand their definitions as presented by Maturidi.
Qadariyya are defined as those who deny predestination from Allah.
3
Examine the hadith as the main evidence
The hadith states that two groups will not receive intercession: Qadariyya and Murji'a. Maturidi uses this to show that the name Qadariyya is authentically established, not a polemical invention.
This hadith is the key to the refutation.
4
Reflect on the naming principle
Maturidi criticizes those who name based on frequency of speech (like calling Mu'tazila Jabriyya) and emphasizes that names should be based on doctrinal reality.
This principle is stated explicitly in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قال من العرب فيجب أن يكون المعتزلة لهم اسم الجبرية لكثرة ما يجري على لسانهم اسم الجبر . ولا قوة إلا بالله . مع ما نسب إلى المجوس ، وهم لا بكثرة القول سموا به، ولكن بحقيقة المذهب. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم سئل عن وجه تسمية الحشوية لهم قدرية، فزعم أن ذا من خطئهم نحو خطئهم في أكثر أمور الدين مع ما انضموا إلى بني مروان، وذلك كان مذهبهم، ليفرحوا بإضافتهم الأفعال الذميمة إلى قضاء الله وقدره. فساعدوهم على ذلك، وبرؤهم عن الذنب بما اقترفوا في الحمل على الله . ورأوا ذلك شائعا لهم ، كفعل معاوية بعمار ، إنه قتله، وقال: قتله على حيث جاء به ، وقولهم : الذي تولى كبره علي وعظم قول عن شرائط الإمامة، حتى قبلوا منهم المعتزلة فيهم حيث أخرجوهم وأطنب في هذا الذي أكثره كذب . قال الفقيه : أما نسبة التسمية إلى الحشوية فإنما هو تمويههم ليروا أن الذي سماهم بهذا هم، وإنما هذه النسبة متوارثة في الأمة بأسرها، في خبر عن النبي عليه السلام: «صنفان من أمتي لا تنالهم شفاعتي: القدرية والمرجئة»، وفسرت القدرية بنفيهم القدر عن الله.
And what was said by the Arabs, then it must be that the Mu'tazila have the name Jabriyya due to the abundance of what occurs on their tongues of the name al-jabr. And there is no power except with Allah. Along with what is attributed to the Magians, but they are not named by the frequency of speech, but by the reality of the doctrine. And there is no power except with Allah. Then he was asked about the reason for the Hashwiyya naming them Qadariyya, so he claimed that this is from their error like their error in most matters of the religion along with their joining Banu Umayya, and that was their doctrine, to rejoice in attributing blameworthy actions to Allah's decree and predestination. So they assisted them in that, and absolved them of sin by what they committed in placing the burden on Allah. And they saw that widespread among them, like the action of Mu'awiya with 'Ammar, indeed he killed him, and said: he killed him for what he brought, and their saying: the one who undertook the bulk of it is 'Ali, and great saying about the conditions of the imamate, until the Mu'tazila accepted from them in them where they expelled them, and he expatiated in this, most of which is false. The jurist said: As for the attribution of the name to the Hashwiyya, it is only their obfuscation so that they see that the one who named them with this is them, but this attribution is inherited in the entire community, in a report from the Prophet peace be upon him: 'Two groups of my community will not attain my intercession: the Qadariyya and the Murji'a', and the Qadariyya are interpreted as those who negate predestination (al-qadar) from Allah.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage discusses the polemical naming of sects. Maturidi begins by stating that the Mu'tazila should be called Jabriyya because they frequently use the term 'jabr' (compulsion) in their discourse. He emphasizes that names are based on doctrinal reality, not just frequency of speech. Then he addresses the claim that the Hashwiyya (a term for certain traditionalists) named them Qadariyya. He says this claim is part of their error, as they also err in other religious matters and allied with Banu Umayya to attribute blameworthy acts to Allah's decree, thereby absolving sinners. He cites the incident of Mu'awiya killing 'Ammar as an example. Then he reports a hadith that two groups will not receive intercession: Qadariyya and Murji'a, and explains that Qadariyya are those who deny that predestination comes from Allah. The overall point is that the names are established by the tradition, not by the opponents.

Key Terms
جبرية
قدرية
حشوية
مرجئة
قدر
شفاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the names of sects are arbitrary or based on mere frequency of speech.
Correction: Maturidi argues that names derive from doctrinal positions, and the name 'Qadariyya' is established by prophetic hadith, not by opponents.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why should the Mu'tazila be called Jabriyya?
A) Because they often use the word 'jabr' in their speech
B) Because they deny divine predestination
C) Because they ally with Banu Umayya
D) Because they are followers of Jabr

Key Takeaway
The prophetic hadith establishes that the name Qadariyya refers to those who deny that Allah determines all things, and this name is inherited from the early community, not invented by opponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone with a theological term (e.g., 'Qadari', 'Jabri'), do you base it on careful examination of their actual beliefs or on hearsay and partisan claims? How will you change that after reading this passage?
?
The Prophet warned that Qadariyya and Murji'a will not receive his intercession. What does that warning stir in you about your own stance on Allah's decree? Are you certain you do not fall into denying qadar in any form?
?
Maturidi distinguishes between names based on doctrine versus names based on frequency of speech. In your own life, are you more concerned with the 'labels' people use about you, or with the truth of your beliefs before Allah? What action will you take to align your heart with doctrine, not just speech?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila and the Banu Marwan Story
Maturidi refutes the story about Banu Umayya and accuses Mu'tazila of sharing with atheists in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Banu Marwan story and his accusation that the Mu'tazila share with atheists in the origination of the world.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila and atheists both distort true understanding of creation and predestination.

Maturidi here confronts two related errors: the false story about Banu Marwan that absolves sinners by blaming predestination, and the Mu'tazila's claim about creating the world. He sees both as leading to slander and confusion in religion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the strong language Maturidi uses.
The passage is dense with accusation and refutation.
2
Identify the two accusations
Find the accusation against the Mu'tazila (sharing with atheists) and the dismissal of the Banu Marwan story.
These are the two main points.
3
Understand Maturidi's dismissal of the story
Maturidi calls the story 'all of it false' and seeks refuge from confusion that leads to slandering Muslims.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why Maturidi would address these issues together and what they reveal about his theological concerns.
This connects to broader debates on predestination and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة فهم شاركوا الملحدة في إنشاء العالم وإخراجه من العدم إلى الوجود. وما ذكر من السبب، وروى عن بني مروان، وحكى عن الذين برأوا المذنبين وحملوا ذلك على ما ذكر في إيجاب القدر للعباد كذب كله فنعوذ بالله من الحيرة في الدين الحاملة على قذف المسلمين.
And as for the Mu'tazila, they shared with the atheists in the origination of the world and bringing it out of non-existence into existence. And what was mentioned of the reason, and narrated from Banu Marwan, and related from those who absolved the sinners and placed that on what was mentioned in the obligation of predestination for the servants, all of it is false. So we seek refuge with Allah from confusion in religion that leads to slandering Muslims.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Maturidi strongly condemns the Mu'tazila, accusing them of sharing the belief of atheists (ملحدة) regarding the creation of the world from nothing. He then dismisses as completely false the stories about Banu Marwan and the claim that some absolved sinners by attributing obligatory predestination to the servants. He concludes by seeking refuge from religious confusion that leads to slandering fellow Muslims.

Key Terms
معتزلة
ملحدة
إنشاء العالم
قدر
قذف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila position on creation is identical to Islamic orthodoxy.
Correction: Maturidi refutes this as a share in atheism, showing it is not orthodox.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi accuse the Mu'tazila of sharing with atheists?
A) Denial of prophethood
B) Origination of the world from nothing
C) Belief in free will
D) Rejection of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Banu Marwan story as false and accuses the Mu'tazila of aligning with atheists on creation, urging protection from confusion that leads to slander.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever uncritically accepted a story about religious figures or events without verifying its authenticity? How might that have influenced your beliefs or actions? Will you commit to investigating the sources of religious narratives before spreading them?
?
Maturidi warns that confusion in religion can lead to slandering fellow Muslims. Think of a time when you misunderstood someone's theological position and spoke ill of them. How can you guard your tongue and seek clarity before judging others?
?
Consider your own understanding of God's role in creating the universe and human responsibility. Are there any subtle influences from non-Islamic worldviews in your thinking? What steps can you take to purify your theology to be solely based on revelation?

Defense of the Hashwiyya against Blanket Accusation
Maturidi dismisses blanket accusations against Hashwiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi defends the Hashwiyya traditionalists from wholesale accusation, insisting that error is individual and not group-wide.

Core Idea
No group is entirely free from error; blame only the guilty.

This passage addresses a polemical accusation against the Hashwiyya traditionalist group. Maturidi argues that attributing error to the entire group is unjust, as no one is immune to mistake. Instead, the fault lies only with those individuals who actually committed it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the structure: first, a statement of fact; then, a qualification.
The word 'ولكن' is implied in the contrast.
2
Identify Maturidi's defense strategy
He does not deny that some Hashwiyya made errors, but rejects generalisation. He uses the principle that no one is free from error.
This reflects a fair and balanced approach in polemics.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this principle applies to judging any group, not just historical sects.
This lesson connects to the Islamic ethic of avoiding slander.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونسب إلى الحشوية الخطأ، ولا أحد سلم عنه. والذي قاله إنما قال قوم منهم. وأما المعتزلة فهم شاركوا الملحدة في إنشاء العالم وإخراجه من العدم إلى الوجود. وما ذكر من السبب، وروى عن بني مروان، وحكى عن الذين برأوا المذنبين وحملوا ذلك على ما ذكر في إيجاب القدر للعباد كذب كله فنعوذ بالله من الحيرة في الدين الحاملة على قذف المسلمين.
And error was attributed to the Hashwiyya, but no one is safe from it. Rather, what was said [against them] was said by some of them. As for the Mu'tazila, they shared with the heretics in the creation of the world and bringing it forth from non-existence into existence. And what was mentioned of the reason, narrated from Banu Marwan, and related from those who acquitted the sinners and interpreted that as necessitating predestination for the servants—all of it is lies. So we seek refuge in God from confusion in religion that leads to slander of Muslims.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses accusations and defends the Hashwiyya (a traditionalist group) from blanket condemnation, noting that the error attributed to them was only from some. Then he strongly criticizes the Mu'tazila for sharing with heretics (mulhida) the belief in creating the world and bringing it from non-existence, meaning they attribute creative power to secondary causes. He then dismisses as lies the report that some acquitted sinners and attributed that to predestination. He ends with a prayer for refuge from confusion.

Key Terms
الحشوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Hashwiyya are blameworthy for errors made by some.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the error was only from some, and no one is completely free from error.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi defend the Hashwiyya against the accusation of error?
A) He denies that any Hashwiyya made an error.
B) He says no one is immune to error, and only some were at fault.
C) He blames the Mu'tazila for slander.
D) He says the accusations were all lies.

Key Takeaway
General blame is unjust; error is individual, not collective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a Muslim group you have dismissed because of the mistakes of some of its members. Can you name that group and the specific error you associated with all of them? How does this lesson challenge you to separate individuals from the group? What concrete step will you take this week to view that group more justly?
?
Recall a time when you were blamed for something others in your community did. How did that feel? The passage says 'no one is safe from error.' In light of that, is there a person or group you have been labeling unfairly? Commit now to remove that label and to speak of them only as individuals.
?
The passage separates error from the entire group. Is there a person in your life whose entire character you have judged based on one mistake? What would it look like to forgive that mistake and see them as a whole person? Write down one thing you can do today to repair that judgment.

Critique of the Mu'tazila for Heretical Agreement
Maturidi criticizes Mu'tazila for heretical leanings and false reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi accuses the Mu'tazila of sharing the heretical belief in creation from non-existence and rejects their attribution of predestination to God as lies.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila agreed with heretics on creation and spread false reports.

In this passage, Maturidi moves from defending the Hashwiyya to attacking the Mu'tazila. He accuses them of aligning with the heretics (mulhida) on the issue of creation, and dismisses as false a report that some acquitted sinners and linked that to predestination. He ends with a prayer for guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage for context
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how Maturidi transitions from the previous topic to this one.
The word 'أما' signals a shift.
2
Understand the accusation against Mu'tazila
Maturidi says they shared with heretics in 'inshā' al-‘ālam' — creating the world from non-existence. This likely refers to their belief in secondary causation or human agency in creation.
The term 'mulhida' refers to atheists or those who deviate from true religion.
3
Analyze the rejection of the predestination report
He dismisses as lies a report from Banu Marwan that some acquitted sinners and used predestination as excuse.
This shows Maturidi's concern with correct attribution of actions to God.
4
Reflect on the final supplication
The prayer 'we seek refuge in God from confusion in religion' shows the danger of such errors.
This highlights the purpose of theology: preserving clarity.

Refutation of the Jabriyya: The Absurdity of Denying Human Agency
Maturidi refutes the Jabriyya by exposing absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Jabriyya position on human action leads to theological absurdities and contradicts divine wisdom.

Core Idea
If all acts are from Allah, then commands and punishments become meaningless.

Imam al-Maturidi takes on the Jabriyya, a group who claimed that all actions, good and evil, are directly from Allah with no human involvement. He shows that this view makes a mockery of divine commands, prophethood, and even Allah's own attributes of justice and wisdom. This passage is a powerful logical dismantling of a position that seems pious but actually undermines everything we know about Allah's relationship with creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Jabriyya claim
Read the passage to see how Maturidi describes the Jabriyya: they title themselves with jabr, attribute all actions to Allah, and deny any actual human act.
2
Trace the absurd consequences
Maturidi shows that if all acts are from Allah, then His commands and prohibitions are directed at Himself, He disobeys and punishes Himself, contradicting His wisdom and mercy.
Focus on the rhetorical questions: 'Why did you do this?' etc.
3
Consider the broader implications
The Jabriyya position invalidates the purpose of messengers, books, and the wisdom of creation, leading to futility.
4
Appreciate the rhetorical force
Maturidi likens those who attribute evil to Allah to a cursed devil, emphasizing the severity of the error.
5
Note the final analogy
The Jabriyya view is compared to the claim that Allah was not knowing or powerful and became so—a clear absurdity.
This highlights the inconsistency of their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجبرية المعروفة عندنا هم الذين تلقبوا بالجبر، وأحالوا القدرة - على ما في الفعل جَعْلُ الله كاذبًا ـ وأرجوا جميع الأفعال إلى الله، ولم يثبتوا للعباد في التحقيق فعلاً. قيل: [هل] يقول لهم الله: لم فعلتم ذا ولم لا فعلتم ذا، أو يقول: افعلوا ذا ولا تفعلوا ذا في التحقيق؛ بل إن أمر أو نهى فإنما يأمر في التحقيق نفسه وينهى نفسه؟ ثم هو يرتكب المنهي في التحقيق، ويأمر ويطيع هو في الحقيقة، ثم يعاقب غيره فيعذبه ويثيبه، ونسميه مع حكيما رحميا. جل [عن ذلك] من صفته الرحمة والحكمة. وعلى ذلك يجب أن لا يجدوا الألم في الحقيقة واللذة وتكون حقيقتهما راجعة إلى الله، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. بل يبطل معنى الرسل والكتب لما هي في التحصيل تصير إلى الله بالأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد، لا منه إلى ذلك. ثم يبطل حكمة خلق الخلق ويحصل على العبث [ب] أن كان العلم يبلغ معرفته. ومن يكون خروج فعله على كفران وجحود المنن والكذب في الإخبار، والسفه في الأفعال فهو حقيق أن يكون شيطانا رجيما، فهو كذلك لا ريب فيه. وهو شبيه بقولهم: كان الله غير عالم ولا قادر، ثم صار كذلك؛ فلعل تدبيره الأفعال التي كانت فيما نسب إلى الخلق [قد وجد] في ذلك الوقت، جل الله وتعالى عن
Then the known Jabriyya among us are those who are titled with jabr, and they attributed power – upon what is in the act, making Allah a liar – and they referred all actions to Allah, and did not establish for the servants any actual act. It is said: Does Allah say to them: 'Why did you do this and why did you not do this? Or does He say: Do this and do not do this in reality? Rather, if He commands or forbids, He only commands Himself in reality and forbids Himself? Then He commits the prohibited in reality, and He commands and He Himself obeys in truth, then He punishes another, torments him and rewards him, and we call Him wise and merciful. Glorified is He above that from His attribute of mercy and wisdom. Upon that, they must not find pain in reality and pleasure, and their reality would be attributed to Allah, glory be to Allah above that and exalted. Rather, the meaning of messengers and books becomes invalid because in attainment they revert to Allah by command, prohibition, promise, and threat, not from Him to that. Then the wisdom of creating creation becomes invalid, and it results in futility if that knowledge reaches His knowledge. And whoever's action comes forth as ingratitude, denial of favors, lying in reports, and foolishness in actions, he is worthy to be a cursed devil, and he is so without doubt. And it is similar to their saying: Allah was not knowing nor powerful, then He became so; perhaps His management of the actions that were attributed to the creation existed at that time, glory be to Allah and exalted above.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabriyya (compulsionists) who deny any human agency and attribute all actions directly to Allah. Maturidi states their position: they claim that power is only in Allah, and they nullify actual human acts. He then exposes the absurd consequences: if all acts are from Allah, then when He commands or forbids, He is commanding Himself; He would then be the one who disobeys and punishes Himself, which contradicts His attributes of mercy and wisdom. Moreover, pain and pleasure would be attributed to Allah, invalidating the purpose of messengers and books, as all commands and threats would ultimately be from Him to Himself. This leads to futility in creation. Those who attribute evil actions to Allah are likened to devils. Finally, he compares their view to the claim that Allah was not knowing or powerful and became so – a clear absurdity. The refutation shows that jabr leads to theological nonsense.

Key Terms
الجبرية (Jabriyya)
الجبر (jabr / compulsion)
القدرة (qudra / power)
التحقيق (reality / actuality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Jabriyya position exalts Allah's power and is therefore commendable.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their position actually leads to contradictions in divine justice and wisdom, not exaltation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue is a consequence of the Jabriyya position?
A) It exalts Allah's power.
B) It makes Allah command and disobey Himself.
C) It affirms human free will.
D) It supports the need for prophets.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya denial of human agency leads to logical absurdities that contradict divine wisdom, justice, and mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'It was written for me' or 'Allah willed it'? How does Maturidi's refutation force you to confront the danger of using divine decree as an excuse for your own choices? What will you say to yourself the next time that thought arises?
?
If you truly believed that every good and evil action you do is actually from Allah, how would that change your sense of remorse for your sins and your gratitude for your obedience? After reading this lesson, can you still hold that belief without feeling the weight of its absurdity? What must you change in your understanding of qadar to preserve Allah's justice and your responsibility?
?
Maturidi says that denying human agency makes 'the meaning of messengers and books become invalid.' Think of a specific verse or hadith that commands you to do something. If you believed that command was really from Allah to Himself and not to you, would you feel any obligation to obey it? How does this lesson renew your sense of personal accountability to every command you hear from the Quran and Sunnah?

Maturidi’s Refutation of the Qadariyya Accusation of Jabr
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya accusation of jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi’s rebuttal of the Mu'tazila claim that denying power before the act constitutes jabr, and his counter-argument that the Mu'tazila themselves are actually closer to jabr.

Core Idea
Denying power before the act is not jabr; affirming an act without power is the true jabr.

Maturidi now responds to a key accusation from the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila): that the Maturidi stance on power (denying its presence before the act) implies jabr. In this passage, he shifts the accusation back, arguing that the Mu'tazila are the ones who actually uphold jabr by affirming an act at a moment when the agent has no power. He uses a sharp analogy from sensory perception to drive home the logical inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Accusation
Read the opening sentence where Maturidi acknowledges the Qadariyya's claim that his school holds jabr. Notice his immediate rejection: 'we disassociate from it in creed and statement.'
The Qadariyya here are the Mu'tazila, who were often labelled by their opponents as Qadariyya because of their emphasis on free will.
2
Identify the Core Disagreement
The Mu'tazila justify the accusation by noting that Maturidis deny the existence of power (qudra) before the act. Find this line and understand the point of contention.
3
Analyse Maturidi’s Counter-Claim
Maturidi then inverts the accusation: the Mu'tazila, by affirming an act at a time when there is no power, are closer to jabr. Study his reasoning and the phrase 'actualizing the act at the time of description without power is closer to the meaning of jabr.'
This is the central turn in the argument.
4
Examine the Analogy and Sensory Support
Read the analogy about an act not being imagined in incapacity but being imagined when incapacity is removed. Then study the sensory example of sight and hearing: when sight is lost, perception fails. Maturidi argues similarly for power and choice.
The analogy shows that power is the cause of the act, and without it the act cannot be genuinely chosen.
5
Reflect on the Conclusion
Maturidi closes with 'And there is no power except with Allah.' Consider how this pious phrase reinforces his position that all power ultimately comes from Allah, yet he still affirms genuine human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نَسب القدرية - وهم الذين تلقبوا بالاعتزال ـ الجبر إلينا، على تبرينا عن عقداً وقولاً، لكن كذبهم في هذا نحو كذبهم علينا في اسم القدرية. ثم نذكر أحقنا بذلك في مقابلة المذهبين، ليعلموا جرأة المعتزلة وعظيم سفههم كما بيّنا في القدرية. وادعوا علينا اسم الجبر بإنكارنا كون قدرة الفعل قبل وقته. ثم هم حققوا الفعل في وقت لا قدرة فيه؛ وتحقيق الفعل في وقت الوصف بلا قدرة أقرب إلى معنى الجبر من تحقيقها مع الفعل لمن عقل الجبر والاختيار. ومما يوضح ذلك أن الفعل غير متوهم في حال العجز، ومتوهم وجوده في حال ارتفاع العجز؛ فكان توهمه مع الارتفاع أرفع وأبلغ من توهمه مع الوجود، إذ هو سبب المنع. فكذلك القدرة التي هي سبب الفعل في الحقيقة. ويؤيد ذلك فساد الدرك بالبصر مع ذهابه بما تقدم من البصر، وكذا السمع وعمل كل الحواس. فكذلك كان فساد فعل الاختيار مع العجز، وفقد القدرة أوضح منه مع الوجود. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the Qadariyya – and they are those titled with i'tizal – ascribed jabr to us, while we disassociate from it in creed and statement, but their lie in this is like their lie against us in the name Qadariyya. Then we will mention who is more deserving of that in comparison of the two sects, so that they may know the audacity of the Mu'tazila and the greatness of their folly as we clarified regarding the Qadariyya. And they claimed against us the name of jabr because of our denial of the existence of power of the act before its time. Then they [themselves] actualize the act at a time when there is no power in it; and actualizing the act at the time of description without power is closer to the meaning of jabr than actualizing it with the act, for one who understands jabr and choice. And what clarifies that is that the act is not imagined in the state of incapacity, while its existence is imagined in the state of removal of incapacity; so its imagination with removal is higher and more complete than its imagination with existence, since it is the cause of prevention. Likewise power which is the cause of the act in reality. And that is supported by the corruption of perception by sight when it [the sight] is gone, as preceded concerning sight, and likewise hearing and the operation of all senses. Thus likewise the corruption of the act of choice with incapacity and lack of power is more evident than with existence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) who accuse the Maturidi school of being Jabriyya (determinists). Maturidi rejects this accusation as a lie, similar to their false naming of 'Qadariyya'. He then turns the tables: the Mu'tazila affirm an act at a time when there is no power (qudra) in the agent, which is actually closer to jabr than the position of the Maturidis. To illustrate, he uses an analogy: an act is not conceivable in a state of complete incapacity, but it is conceivable when incapacity is removed. The removal of incapacity is a stronger basis for the possibility of the act than its mere existence. Power is the cause of the act, and when power is absent the act cannot be truly chosen. He compares this to sensory perception: when sight is lost, seeing is impossible; similarly, when power is lost, choice fails. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's position (affirming an act without power at the moment) is more deterministic. He ends with the pious phrase.

Key Terms
القدرية (Qadariyya)
الاعتزال (i'tizal)
الجبر (jabr)
القدرة (qudra)
الاختيار (ikhtiyar / choice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that power exists before the act necessarily leads to jabr (determinism).
Correction: Maturidi argues that the real jabr is affirming an act when there is no power at all, as the Mu'tazila do; his own position preserves genuine choice because power and act are simultaneous.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which group is actually closer to jabr?
A) The Jabriyya
B) The Maturidis
C) The Mu'tazila
D) The Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila, not the Maturidis, are closer to jabr because they affirm an act without power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have accused someone of being fatalistic or passive, have you ever checked whether your own position inadvertently leads to the very determinism you reject? This passage warns against that. In which relationship or belief of yours might you be insisting on a condition that actually undermines the freedom you want to protect?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that power must be before the act. Maturidi says that requiring that actually leads to jabr. Is there a condition you demand from Allah (like understanding every wisdom before trusting) that, if met, would leave you acting without real faith? Are you willing to act with only the power He gives at the moment, without demanding control in advance?
?
Maturidi says an act is not imagined in a state of incapacity, but is imagined when incapacity is removed. What state of incapacity (real or imagined) have you used as an excuse to avoid acting for Allah? This lesson teaches that the very fact you can imagine doing the action is a sign that power from Allah can be present. Will you act today based on that hope, or stay in the illusion of incapacity?

The Verbal Dispute over Power for Faith
Maturidi highlights the nominal nature of the dispute over qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the disagreement between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila regarding the unbeliever's power for faith is essentially verbal: they agree on the underlying meaning but differ in terminology.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila and al-Husayn agree on the meaning but differ on the name.

Maturidi begins by setting aside the Jabriyya criticism and then focuses on the agreement between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila. He shows that both sides accept that the unbeliever lacks divine success (tawfiq) and protection (isma), yet they call this lack by different names. The real issue is not about power itself but about how to label it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic once, then the translation, to understand the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the three groups: Jabriyya, al-Husayn, Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the positions on power
Find what al-Husayn says about the unbeliever's power and what the Mu'tazila agree with.
Al-Husayn denies power for faith; Mu'tazila agree on the lack.
3
Spot the nominal difference
See that both sides agree on the meaning (lack of tawfiq/isma) but call it different names.
The dispute is about terminology, not reality.
4
Reflect on the theological method
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes this point: many disputes are based on labels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علامة القسر والجبر. فعابت الجبرية في جبر العبد بما رأوا الله الملك والجلال، ثم قالت رب العالمين سفها بغير علم ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم نذكر طرفًا مما عابت المعتزلة حسينا في النطق ووافقته في التحصيل. قال الحسين : الكافر وقت كفره ليست له قدرة الإيمان ؛ وقدرة الإيمان عنده التوفيق والعصمة ووافقته المعتزلة على أنه ليس بمعصوم ولا موفق، بل هو مخذول متروك على رأيه، وذلك معنى قدرة الكفر عند الحسين. فاتفقا على المعنى الذي اختلفا في اسمه فحق المسألة بينهم في جعل التوفيق والعصمة قوة الإيمان والترك والخذلان قوة الكفر ، لا [ في] إفراد التكلم في القدرة والإغضاء عن حقيقة ما يجب القول به. وبالله التوفيق. وقال الحسين : معنى الإرادة في الله سبحانه أنه لم يغلب ولم يُقهر». وقد أعطت المعتزلة هذا المعنى في جميع أفعال الخلق : إنه لم يُغلب ولم يُقهر ، فتبطل المسألة في الإرادة؛ إنما بقيت في تأويل الإرادة لا غير. مع ما كان من قول الحسين: «إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة، فأراد خالقها كونها [١٦٩] على ما خلقها». ومذهب المعتزلة أنها ليست بمخلوقة لله، فتكون المسألة في خلق الأفعال / لا في الإرادة. وقال الكعبي : الإرادة معناها أنه مختار غير مغلوب، فمثله في كل شيء يلزمه. ثم المعتزلة ليست تُثبت الله إلى العالم سوى أن كان ولم يكن عالَم، ثم كان عالم ، فصار بذلك المعنى خالقا له، مريداً على الوجه الذي ذُكر . فقال الحسين في أفعال العباد : إنه إذ كان ولا هذه الأفعال، ثم كانت هذه، وكانت بإرادته التي تأويلها ما وصفه، وكان خلقها بأن كان ولم تكن هي . ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أن "الحسين" يجعله في الأول مريداً لكون الخلق على ما كان، وكذلك لكون كل مخلوق على ما كان بإرادته. والمعتزلة تنفي معنى الإرادة، لا تجعل غير أن كان ولم يكن الخلق ثم كان؛ فحق ذلك فيه أوجب. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقالت المعتزلة : الوعيد يأخذ من أخرجه فعله عن الإيمان، وكذلك قال الحسين وجميع أهل الإرجاء : أن كان من استحق بفعله زوال اسم الإيمان فهو كله في النار أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله . والاختلاف بين هؤلاء فيما به يخرج من الإيمان لا في حق الوعيد، فالاحتجاج بآي الوعيد في المسألة خطأ.
The mark of coercion and compulsion. The Jabriyya criticized the compulsion of the servant because they saw God's sovereignty and majesty, then they spoke foolishly about the Lord of the worlds without knowledge, and there is no power except with God. Then we mention a portion of what the Mu'tazila criticized al-Husayn for in expression but agreed with him in outcome. Al-Husayn said: 'The unbeliever at the time of his unbelief has no power for faith; and power for faith according to him is divine success (tawfiq) and protection (isma).' And the Mu'tazila agreed with him that he is neither protected nor divinely successful, but rather abandoned and left to his opinion, and that is the meaning of power for unbelief according to al-Husayn. So they agreed on the meaning while differing on its name; thus the issue between them is about making tawfiq and isma the power of faith, and abandonment and forsaking the power of unbelief, not about isolating speech on power and ignoring the reality of what must be said. And with God is success. And al-Husayn said: 'The meaning of will (irada) in God Almighty is that He is not overcome and not coerced.' And the Mu'tazila have given this meaning in all actions of creation: that He is not overcome and not coerced, so the issue concerning will becomes invalid; it remains only concerning the interpretation of will, nothing else. Along with what al-Husayn said: 'The actions of servants are created, so their Creator willed their existence as He created them.' And the Mu'tazila's doctrine is that they are not created by God, so the issue is about the creation of actions, not will. And al-Ka'bi said: 'Will means that He is free and not overcome, and the like of that is necessary for everything.' Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm God's relation to the world except that He was and there was no world, then the world came to be, so by that meaning He is its Creator, willing in the manner mentioned. So al-Husayn said concerning the actions of servants: 'When there were no such actions, then these came to be, and they came to be by His will whose interpretation is as described, and their creation was that He existed while they did not.' And there is no power except with God. Moreover, al-Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing for the creation to be as it was, and likewise for every created thing to be as it was by His will. And the Mu'tazila deny the meaning of will; they do not posit anything other than that there was no creation and then it came to be; so that matter is more incumbent upon it. And there is no power except with God. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The threat (wa'id) applies to one whose action removes him from faith.' And likewise al-Husayn and all the Murji'a said: 'If someone by his action deserves the removal of the name of faith, then he is entirely in the Fire forever.' And there is no power except with God. And the disagreement between them concerns that by which one leaves faith, not concerning the reality of the threat; so arguing with verses of threat in this issue is an error.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage compares the positions of al-Husayn (likely al-Najjar) and the Mu'tazila on two key issues: power (qudra) and divine will (irada). Maturidi first notes that the Jabriyya criticized based on God's sovereignty. He then focuses on the agreement between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila in outcome despite different wording. Al-Husayn denies the unbeliever power for faith, equating power with tawfiq and isma. The Mu'tazila agree that the unbeliever lacks these, but they call it 'abandonment'. Thus the dispute is merely nominal. On will, Maturidi shows that both sides define God's will as not being coerced, but al-Husayn insists God wills actions as He creates them, while the Mu'tazila deny that God creates actions. The disagreement thus shifts to the creation of acts. Maturidi then cites al-Ka'bi's definition of will as free choice, and concludes that the real difference is over the interpretation of 'will'. Finally, he addresses the threat (wa'id): both agree that whoever loses the name of faith by his action is eternally in Hell, but they differ on what actions cause that loss. Therefore, using verses of threat in argument is misplaced. The overall point is that many disputes are verbal, not substantive.

Key Terms
قدرة
توفيق
عصمة
إيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and al-Husayn disagreed on whether the unbeliever has any power at all.
Correction: Actually, they agreed that the unbeliever lacks tawfiq and isma, but disagreed on whether that lack should be called 'power for faith' or 'power for unbelief'.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the real disagreement between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila regarding the power of the unbeliever?
A) Whether the unbeliever has any power at all
B) The naming of power for faith versus power for unbelief
C) Whether God gives power directly
D) Whether the unbeliever can be forgiven

Key Takeaway
The disagreement over the unbeliever's power for faith is essentially verbal, as both sides affirm the same reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own faith and deeds, do you rely on your own power or do you see your success as entirely from Allah? How does acknowledging His tawfiq change how you approach obedience?
?
Are there areas in your life where you attribute your 'success' to your own ability, forgetting that even your capacity comes from Allah? What would it mean to truly internalize that all power for good is from Him?
?
The lesson shows a dispute over naming the reality of the unbeliever's state. Have you ever been caught up in theological debates that missed the underlying reality? How can you prioritize substance over terminology in your own understanding of faith?

The Real Issue: Creation of Actions, Not the Meaning of Will
Maturidi demonstrates that the will debate reduces to the creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute over divine will between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila masks a deeper disagreement about whether God creates human actions.

Core Idea
Al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree God is not coerced; the real disagreement is about khalq al-af'al.

Maturidi turns to the topic of divine will (irada). Both al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila define God's will as His not being overcome. However, al-Husayn goes on to assert that the actions of servants are created by God, whereas the Mu'tazila deny this. Maturidi shows that the apparent dispute about will is actually about the creation of acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the progression from will to creation.
Note the shift in focus.
2
Identify the initial agreement
Both define God's will as not being coerced.
This makes the dispute about will appear trivial.
3
Notice al-Husayn's additional claim
He says actions are created by God. The Mu'tazila deny this.
This becomes the real point of contention.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi shows that the real issue is khalq al-af'al, not the meaning of irada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The mark of coercion and compulsion. The Jabriyya criticized the compulsion of the servant because they saw God's sovereignty and majesty, then they spoke foolishly about the Lord of the worlds without knowledge, and there is no power except with God. Then we mention a portion of what the Mu'tazila criticized al-Husayn for in expression but agreed with him in outcome. Al-Husayn said: 'The unbeliever at the time of his unbelief has no power for faith; and power for faith according to him is divine success (tawfiq) and protection (isma).' And the Mu'tazila agreed with him that he is neither protected nor divinely successful, but rather abandoned and left to his opinion, and that is the meaning of power for unbelief according to al-Husayn. So they agreed on the meaning while differing on its name; thus the issue between them is about making tawfiq and isma the power of faith, and abandonment and forsaking the power of unbelief, not about isolating speech on power and ignoring the reality of what must be said. And with God is success. And al-Husayn said: 'The meaning of will (irada) in God Almighty is that He is not overcome and not coerced.' And the Mu'tazila have given this meaning in all actions of creation: that He is not overcome and not coerced, so the issue concerning will becomes invalid; it remains only concerning the interpretation of will, nothing else. Along with what al-Husayn said: 'The actions of servants are created, so their Creator willed their existence as He created them.' And the Mu'tazila's doctrine is that they are not created by God, so the issue is about the creation of actions, not will. And al-Ka'bi said: 'Will means that He is free and not overcome, and the like of that is necessary for everything.' Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm God's relation to the world except that He was and there was no world, then the world came to be, so by that meaning He is its Creator, willing in the manner mentioned. So al-Husayn said concerning the actions of servants: 'When there were no such actions, then these came to be, and they came to be by His will whose interpretation is as described, and their creation was that He existed while they did not.' And there is no power except with God. Moreover, al-Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing for the creation to be as it was, and likewise for every created thing to be as it was by His will. And the Mu'tazila deny the meaning of will; they do not posit anything other than that there was no creation and then it came to be; so that matter is more incumbent upon it. And there is no power except with God. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The threat (wa'id) applies to one whose action removes him from faith.' And likewise al-Husayn and all the Murji'a said: 'If someone by his action deserves the removal of the name of faith, then he is entirely in the Fire forever.' And there is no power except with God. And the disagreement between them concerns that by which one leaves faith, not concerning the reality of the threat; so arguing with verses of threat in this issue is an error.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage compares the positions of al-Husayn (likely al-Najjar) and the Mu'tazila on two key issues: power (qudra) and divine will (irada). Maturidi first notes that the Jabriyya criticized based on God's sovereignty. He then focuses on the agreement between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila in outcome despite different wording. Al-Husayn denies the unbeliever power for faith, equating power with tawfiq and isma. The Mu'tazila agree that the unbeliever lacks these, but they call it 'abandonment'. Thus the dispute is merely nominal. On will, Maturidi shows that both sides define God's will as not being coerced, but al-Husayn insists God wills actions as He creates them, while the Mu'tazila deny that God creates actions. The disagreement thus shifts to the creation of acts. Maturidi then cites al-Ka'bi's definition of will as free choice, and concludes that the real difference is over the interpretation of 'will'. Finally, he addresses the threat (wa'id): both agree that whoever loses the name of faith by his action is eternally in Hell, but they differ on what actions cause that loss. Therefore, using verses of threat in argument is misplaced. The overall point is that many disputes are verbal, not substantive.

خلق الأفعال
مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and al-Husayn disagreed on the meaning of God's will.
Correction: They agreed that God's will means He is not coerced; their disagreement is about whether God creates actions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what becomes the actual point of contention between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila after they agree on the meaning of will?
A) Whether God is free from coercion
B) Whether human actions are created by God
C) Whether the world is created
D) Whether the Qur'an is created

Key Takeaway
The dispute over divine will is actually about the creation of human actions, not about the definition of will itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a theological debate can appear to be about one thing (the meaning of will) but actually be about something deeper (creation of actions). When you argue with others about religious matters, do you tend to focus on terminology or on the core issue? What would it take for you to dig deeper and identify the real point of contention, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
If God's will truly means He is 'not overcome or coerced,' how does that affect your understanding of your own choices and actions? Do you live as though your actions are fully your own, or as though they are entirely created by God—and what is the evidence in your daily life for either?
?
The Mu'tazila and al-Husayn agreed on the meaning of will yet parted ways on the creation of actions. In your own life, are there disagreements where you actually share common ground but refuse to see it? Could admitting that common ground force you to change your position on something you hold dear? What would that change cost you?

The Threat: Agreement on the Consequence, Disagreement on the Cause
Maturidi shows that the wa'id debate is about the cause of unbelief, not the punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Mu'tazila and al-Husayn (and the Murji'a) agree that those who lose the name of faith are eternally in Hell, but they disagree on what actions cause the loss of faith.

Core Idea
Both sides agree on the punishment; the disagreement is about the threshold of leaving faith.

Maturidi concludes by addressing the topic of the divine threat (wa'id). He points out that both the Mu'tazila and al-Husayn (and the Murji'a) agree that if someone's action removes the name of faith from him, he is eternally in the Fire. The disagreement is only over which specific actions cause that removal. Therefore, arguing with verses of threat in this context is a mistake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic and translation to see the agreement on wa'id.
Both sides agree on eternal punishment for those who lose the name of faith.
2
Find the point of disagreement
Maturidi says they differ on what causes removal from faith.
The punishment is not in dispute.
3
Understand the methodological error
Using verses of threat is irrelevant because the parties do not disagree on the threat itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علامة القسر والجبر. فعابت الجبرية في جبر العبد بما رأوا الله الملك والجلال، ثم قالت رب العالمين سفها بغير علم ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم نذكر طرفًا مما عابت المعتزلة حسينا في النطق ووافقته في التحصيل. قال الحسين : الكافر وقت كفره ليست له قدرة الإيمان ؛ وقدرة الإيمان عنده التوفيق والعصمة ووافقته المعتزلة على أنه ليس بمعصوم ولا موفق، بل هو مخذول متروك على رأيه، وذلك معنى قدرة الكفر عند الحسين. فاتفقا على المعنى الذي اختلفا في اسمه فحق المسألة بينهم في جعل التوفيق والعصمة قوة الإيمان والترك والخذلان قوة الكفر ، لا [ في] إفراد التكلم في القدرة والإغضاء عن حقيقة ما يجب القول به. وبالله التوفيق. وقال الحسين : معنى الإرادة في الله سبحانه أنه لم يغلب ولم يُقهر». وقد أعطت المعتزلة هذا المعنى في جميع أفعال الخلق : إنه لم يُغلب ولم يُقهر ، فتبطل المسألة في الإرادة؛ إنما بقيت في تأويل الإرادة لا غير. مع ما كان من قول الحسين: «إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة، فأراد خالقها كونها [١٦٩] على ما خلقها». ومذهب المعتزلة أنها ليست بمخلوقة لله، فتكون المسألة في خلق الأفعال / لا في الإرادة. وقال الكعبي : الإرادة معناها أنه مختار غير مغلوب، فمثله في كل شيء يلزمه. ثم المعتزلة ليست تُثبت الله إلى العالم سوى أن كان ولم يكن عالَم، ثم كان عالم ، فصار بذلك المعنى خالقا له، مريداً على الوجه الذي ذُكر . فقال الحسين في أفعال العباد : إنه إذ كان ولا هذه الأفعال، ثم كانت هذه، وكانت بإرادته التي تأويلها ما وصفه، وكان خلقها بأن كان ولم تكن هي . ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أن "الحسين" يجعله في الأول مريداً لكون الخلق على ما كان، وكذلك لكون كل مخلوق على ما كان بإرادته. والمعتزلة تنفي معنى الإرادة، لا تجعل غير أن كان ولم يكن الخلق ثم كان؛ فحق ذلك فيه أوجب. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقالت المعتزلة : الوعيد يأخذ من أخرجه فعله عن الإيمان، وكذلك قال الحسين وجميع أهل الإرجاء : أن كان من استحق بفعله زوال اسم الإيمان فهو كله في النار أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله . والاختلاف بين هؤلاء فيما به يخرج من الإيمان لا في حق الوعيد، فالاحتجاج بآي الوعيد في المسألة خطأ.
The mark of coercion and compulsion. The Jabriyya criticized the compulsion of the servant because they saw God's sovereignty and majesty, then they spoke foolishly about the Lord of the worlds without knowledge, and there is no power except with God. Then we mention a portion of what the Mu'tazila criticized al-Husayn for in expression but agreed with him in outcome. Al-Husayn said: 'The unbeliever at the time of his unbelief has no power for faith; and power for faith according to him is divine success (tawfiq) and protection (isma).' And the Mu'tazila agreed with him that he is neither protected nor divinely successful, but rather abandoned and left to his opinion, and that is the meaning of power for unbelief according to al-Husayn. So they agreed on the meaning while differing on its name; thus the issue between them is about making tawfiq and isma the power of faith, and abandonment and forsaking the power of unbelief, not about isolating speech on power and ignoring the reality of what must be said. And with God is success. And al-Husayn said: 'The meaning of will (irada) in God Almighty is that He is not overcome and not coerced.' And the Mu'tazila have given this meaning in all actions of creation: that He is not overcome and not coerced, so the issue concerning will becomes invalid; it remains only concerning the interpretation of will, nothing else. Along with what al-Husayn said: 'The actions of servants are created, so their Creator willed their existence as He created them.' And the Mu'tazila's doctrine is that they are not created by God, so the issue is about the creation of actions, not will. And al-Ka'bi said: 'Will means that He is free and not overcome, and the like of that is necessary for everything.' Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm God's relation to the world except that He was and there was no world, then the world came to be, so by that meaning He is its Creator, willing in the manner mentioned. So al-Husayn said concerning the actions of servants: 'When there were no such actions, then these came to be, and they came to be by His will whose interpretation is as described, and their creation was that He existed while they did not.' And there is no power except with God. Moreover, al-Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing for the creation to be as it was, and likewise for every created thing to be as it was by His will. And the Mu'tazila deny the meaning of will; they do not posit anything other than that there was no creation and then it came to be; so that matter is more incumbent upon it. And there is no power except with God. And the Mu'tazila said: 'The threat (wa'id) applies to one whose action removes him from faith.' And likewise al-Husayn and all the Murji'a said: 'If someone by his action deserves the removal of the name of faith, then he is entirely in the Fire forever.' And there is no power except with God. And the disagreement between them concerns that by which one leaves faith, not concerning the reality of the threat; so arguing with verses of threat in this issue is an error.

Commentary
This passage compares the positions of al-Husayn (likely al-Najjar) and the Mu'tazila on two key issues: power (qudra) and divine will (irada). Maturidi first notes that the Jabriyya criticized based on God's sovereignty. He then focuses on the agreement between al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila in outcome despite different wording. Al-Husayn denies the unbeliever power for faith, equating power with tawfiq and isma. The Mu'tazila agree that the unbeliever lacks these, but they call it 'abandonment'. Thus the dispute is merely nominal. On will, Maturidi shows that both sides define God's will as not being coerced, but al-Husayn insists God wills actions as He creates them, while the Mu'tazila deny that God creates actions. The disagreement thus shifts to the creation of acts. Maturidi then cites al-Ka'bi's definition of will as free choice, and concludes that the real difference is over the interpretation of 'will'. Finally, he addresses the threat (wa'id): both agree that whoever loses the name of faith by his action is eternally in Hell, but they differ on what actions cause that loss. Therefore, using verses of threat in argument is misplaced. The overall point is that many disputes are verbal, not substantive.

?

Refuting Mu'tazila and Khawarij on the Status of Sinners
Maturidi refutes both Mu'tazila and Khawarij using Quranic commands to repent without removing faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic commands to repent to refute both the Mu'tazila's removal of faith for major sins and the Khawarij's excommunication of sinners.

Core Idea
The Quran commands believers to repent, proving a sinner remains a believer.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument based on two Quranic verses where Allah commands believers to repent while still addressing them as believers. This simple fact exposes a flaw in both the Mu'tazila and Khawarij positions. Let's examine how.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses cited
Read the two verses Maturidi quotes (24:31 and 66:8) carefully. Notice that Allah addresses the sinners as 'believers' (al-Mu'minun) while commanding them to repent.
The verses are: 'And turn to Allah all together, O believers, that you may succeed' and 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance.'
2
Understand Maturidi's inference
Maturidi argues that since Allah commands repentance while maintaining the title of faith, the person must still be considered a believer. Repentance is for forgiveness, not for regaining faith.
The key point: faith and sin coexist; sin does not negate faith.
3
See how this refutes the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claim that every major sin removes the name of faith until repentance. But here the address is to 'believers', so the name remains even before repentance.
The verse contradicts their position by affirming faith alongside the command to repent.
4
See how this refutes the Khawarij
The Khawarij label major sinners as disbelievers (kuffar) and polytheists. Yet the Quran commands them to repent while still calling them believers – an impossibility if they were actually disbelievers.
The command implies they are still believers, not outside the fold.
5
Reflect on the middle position
Maturidi affirms that a person who commits a major sin remains a believer, but is sinful and must repent. This is the moderate Sunni position between the extremes.
This lesson shows how Quranic reasoning resolves theological disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى قال في الذنوب التي لا يغفرها سواء عليهم أستغفرت الآية، وعلى ذلك قال: وتوبوا إلى الله جميعا أيها المؤمنون لعلكم تفلحون، وقال: يا أيها الذين آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا الآية، فألزمهم التوبة مع إثبات الإيمان، وأخبر أنه بالتوبة يغفر لهم. وفي ذلك وجهان أحدهما على المعتزلة في إزالتهم اسم الإيمان في كل ذنب لا يُغفر عندهم إلا بالتوبة، وفي ذلك إثباته، وعلى الخوارج بتسميتهم كفرة وأهل الشرك، ومحال مع ذلك اسم الإيمان والأمر بغيره. والله الموفق.
And also Allah the Exalted said concerning sins that He does not forgive: 'It is the same for them whether you ask forgiveness' the verse, and upon that He said: 'And turn to Allah all together, O believers, that you may succeed' (24:31), and He said: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance' the verse. So He obligated them to repent while affirming faith, and informed that through repentance He forgives them. In that there are two aspects: one against the Mu'tazila in their removal of the name of faith for every sin that is not forgiven except with repentance – and in that is its affirmation; and against the Khawarij in their labeling [such people] disbelievers and people of shirk – and it is impossible with that [label] to have the name of faith and the command to something else. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of both the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij based on Quranic commands. Maturidi cites two verses where Allah commands believers to repent while still addressing them as 'believers' (al-Mu'minun). The first is from Surah an-Nur (24:31), and the second is from Surah at-Tahrim (66:8). By commanding repentance while maintaining the title of faith, Allah shows that a person who commits a sin does not lose the name of believer entirely. Maturidi then extracts two implications: first, against the Mu'tazila who say that every major sin removes the name of faith until repentance – but the verse affirms faith alongside the command to repent, contradicting their position. Second, against the Khawarij who declare such sinners disbelievers and polytheists – but it is impossible to command someone to repent while simultaneously calling them a disbeliever, since the command implies they are still believers. Thus both extreme positions are refuted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who commits a major sin is no longer a believer (Mu'tazila view) or becomes a disbeliever (Khawarij view).
Correction: The Quran commands such sinners to repent while still calling them believers, proving they remain believers.

Checkpoint
What two groups are refuted by the Quranic command to repent while addressing believers as believers?
A) Mu'tazila and Khawarij
B) Ash'aris and Maturidis
C) Jahmiyya and Qadariyya
D) Shi'a and Zaydis

Key Takeaway
The Quran consistently addresses believers even when commanding repentance, proving that major sins do not negate faith entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
What sin in your life have you neglected to repent from because you felt you were not worthy of being called a believer? Will you now, based on this lesson, repent while embracing the identity that Allah has given you as a believer?

Refutation of Khawarij: Minor Sins and Prophetic Example
Maturidi refutes Khawarij by reductio ad absurdum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Khawarij by showing that their view of minor sins as disbelief would absurdly deem prophets and saints as disbelievers.

Core Idea
If minor sins cause disbelief, prophets would be disbelievers – an impossible conclusion.

The Khawarij were known for their extreme position that committing minor sins makes a person a disbeliever. Maturidi here demonstrates the flaw in their reasoning by applying it to prophets and saints, who themselves had slips but remained believers. This argument reveals the contradiction at the heart of the Khawarij's extreme view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Khawarij claim
Read the passage and note what the Khawarij believe about minor sins and disbelief.
They hold that any sin, even minor, leads to disbelief.
2
Trace Maturidi's reasoning
Maturidi points out that prophets and awliya were afflicted with sins. If the Khawarij view were true, they would be disbelievers, which nullifies prophethood and sainthood.
3
Reflect on the absurd consequence
Consider how describing a prophet as a believer despite sins would be considered disbelief by the Khawarij. This shows the extremism leads to a greater sin.
Maturidi calls this 'the greatest of sins'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض قول الخوارج الذين يكفرون بالصغائر ما بلى به الأنبياء والأولياء، وما يكفر به يسقط النبوة والولاية. ومن ذلك وصف إيمانه بالأنبياء فهو كافر بهم. فبلغ من تعظيمهم الذنوب إلى أن كفروا به، وهو أعظم الذنوب. وهذا حق من تعدى حدود الله في الحكم وغلا في دين الله: أن يكون عطبه في أرجى ما يكون عنده من أسباب النجاة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى قول المعتزلة في ذلك وصف الله الأنبياء بالدعاء له تضرعا وخفية وطمعا وخوفا وببكائهم على ما كان منهم من الزلات وتضرعهم إليه حتى أجيبوا في دعائهم وأعطوا.
Then what refutes the statement of the Khawarij, who declare [people] disbelievers for minor sins, is what the prophets and awliya were afflicted with, and what they declare disbelief thereby nullifies prophethood and sainthood. And from that is that describing the prophets by faith [in them], he would be a disbeliever in them. So their magnifying of sins reached such an extent that they declared [the prophets] disbelievers, and that is the greatest of sins. And this is the truth of one who transgresses the limits of Allah in judgment and goes to extremes in the religion of Allah: that his destruction lies in the most hopeful thing in his view among the means of salvation. And there is no power except with Allah. And according to the Mu'tazila in that matter, Allah described the prophets as supplicating to Him humbly and secretly, with hope and fear, and with their weeping over the slips that occurred from them, and their imploring Him until they were answered in their supplication and they were given.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a dual refutation: first against the Khawarij, then against the Mu'tazila. Maturidi argues that if minor sins (sagha'ir) were enough to make a person a disbeliever, then the prophets and the awliya (saints) would be disbelievers, because they too committed slips and sins. This would nullify prophethood and sainthood. He points out an absurd consequence: if one describes a prophet by his faith (i.e., that he had faith despite his sin), then according to the Khawarij that person would be a disbeliever in the prophet because he does not consider the sin as kufr. This shows the extremism of the Khawarij: they magnified sins so much that they ended up declaring even prophets disbelievers, which is itself the greatest sin. Such a person is one who oversteps the limits of Allah. Then Maturidi turns to the Mu'tazila, who deny that prophets have any sin at all (the doctrine of 'isma). He cites Quranic descriptions of prophets supplicating with humility, fear, and hope, and weeping over their faults, and their supplications being answered. This contradicts the Mu'tazila claim that prophets are sinless, because the prophets themselves acknowledged their errors and asked for forgiveness. Thus both extreme views are refuted.

Key Terms
أنبياء
أولياء
ذنوب
صغائر
نبوة
ولاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin, even minor, makes a person a disbeliever.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prophets and awliya had sins, yet they remained believers, so minor sins do not cause disbelief.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Maturidi draw from the Khawarij view that minor sins cause disbelief?
A) Prophets would be considered disbelievers, nullifying prophethood
B) Only major sins cause disbelief
C) No one can be a believer
D) Sins are automatically forgiven

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij's extreme view on minor sins leads to the absurd conclusion that prophets are disbelievers, proving their error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered someone a disbeliever for a sin they committed, even a minor one? Pause and reflect: How does the Khawarij's error mirror your own heart when you judge others harshly? Will you repent from this tendency and instead leave judgment to Allah?
?
The Khawarij became so focused on sins that they ended up declaring prophets as disbelievers. Is there an area of your life where your fear of sin has made you extreme, turning you away from mercy and compassion? What step can you take today to return to the balanced path of the Sunnah?
?
The passage says the Khawarij's destruction lies in what they thought most hopeful – their extremism. What 'hopeful' practice of yours might actually be leading you away from Allah? How can you check your intentions against the teachings of Ahl al-Sunna?

Refutation of Mu'tazila: Prophetic Supplications and Tears
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila using Quranic verses on prophetic supplications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic descriptions of prophets' humble supplications and tears to refute the Mu'tazila claim that prophets are absolutely sinless.

Core Idea
Prophets wept over their slips – a clear sign they had faults.

The Mu'tazila held that prophets must be completely free from sin, even minor ones, to preserve trust in their message. Maturidi counters this by pointing to the Quran where prophets are described as supplicating humbly, weeping over their errors, and being answered. This shows that prophets themselves acknowledged their faults.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila believe prophets are sinless. Read the passage to see their stance.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Maturidi cites Allah describing prophets as supplicating humbly, secretly, with hope and fear, and weeping over their slips. This implies they had slips to weep over.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since the prophets wept over their sins and were answered, they must have committed sins. This refutes the Mu'tazila claim of absolute sinlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض قول الخوارج الذين يكفرون بالصغائر ما بلى به الأنبياء والأولياء، وما يكفر به يسقط النبوة والولاية. ومن ذلك وصف إيمانه بالأنبياء فهو كافر بهم. فبلغ من تعظيمهم الذنوب إلى أن كفروا به، وهو أعظم الذنوب. وهذا حق من تعدى حدود الله في الحكم وغلا في دين الله: أن يكون عطبه في أرجى ما يكون عنده من أسباب النجاة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى قول المعتزلة في ذلك وصف الله الأنبياء بالدعاء له تضرعا وخفية وطمعا وخوفا وببكائهم على ما كان منهم من الزلات وتضرعهم إليه حتى أجيبوا في دعائهم وأعطوا.
Then what refutes the statement of the Khawarij, who declare [people] disbelievers for minor sins, is what the prophets and awliya were afflicted with, and what they declare disbelief thereby nullifies prophethood and sainthood. And from that is that describing the prophets by faith [in them], he would be a disbeliever in them. So their magnifying of sins reached such an extent that they declared [the prophets] disbelievers, and that is the greatest of sins. And this is the truth of one who transgresses the limits of Allah in judgment and goes to extremes in the religion of Allah: that his destruction lies in the most hopeful thing in his view among the means of salvation. And there is no power except with Allah. And according to the Mu'tazila in that matter, Allah described the prophets as supplicating to Him humbly and secretly, with hope and fear, and with their weeping over the slips that occurred from them, and their imploring Him until they were answered in their supplication and they were given.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a dual refutation: first against the Khawarij, then against the Mu'tazila. Maturidi argues that if minor sins (sagha'ir) were enough to make a person a disbeliever, then the prophets and the awliya (saints) would be disbelievers, because they too committed slips and sins. This would nullify prophethood and sainthood. He points out an absurd consequence: if one describes a prophet by his faith (i.e., that he had faith despite his sin), then according to the Khawarij that person would be a disbeliever in the prophet because he does not consider the sin as kufr. This shows the extremism of the Khawarij: they magnified sins so much that they ended up declaring even prophets disbelievers, which is itself the greatest sin. Such a person is one who oversteps the limits of Allah. Then Maturidi turns to the Mu'tazila, who deny that prophets have any sin at all (the doctrine of 'isma). He cites Quranic descriptions of prophets supplicating with humility, fear, and hope, and weeping over their faults, and their supplications being answered. This contradicts the Mu'tazila claim that prophets are sinless, because the prophets themselves acknowledged their errors and asked for forgiveness. Thus both extreme views are refuted.

Key Terms
زلات
دعاء
تضرع
بكاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets are completely sinless and never made any mistakes.
Correction: The Quran shows prophets wept over their slips and sought forgiveness, indicating they had faults.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Maturidi use to refute the Mu'tazila claim of prophetic sinlessness?
A) Prophets committed major sins
B) Prophets wept over their slips and supplicated humbly
C) Prophets never made mistakes
D) The Quran does not mention prophetic sins

Key Takeaway
The Quran's description of prophets weeping over their slips contradicts the Mu'tazila claim of absolute sinlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophets wept over their slips. When was the last time you wept over your own faults before Allah? What keeps you from that level of humility?
?
If the best of creation needed to supplicate secretly with hope and fear, what does your prayer life reveal about your recognition of your own shortcomings?
?
You know you have slips and sins. Will you now take a moment to name one specific fault and turn to Allah with tears, just as the prophets did?
