The Linguistic Meaning of Irja' and Refutation of the Hashwiyya
Maturidi explains irja' as postponement and refutes Hashwiyya's naming claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's linguistic argument that the term 'irja'' means postponement and why the Hashwiyya's claim about the Murji'ah's naming is linguistically and rationally invalid.

Core Idea
Irja' means postponement, not a theological stance on faith and deeds.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a fundamental linguistic point: what does the term 'irja'' actually mean? He establishes that all Arabic speakers agree it means 'postponement,' as shown in the Quran. Then he confronts the Hashwiyya sect, who claimed the Murji'ah were named because they excluded deeds from faith. Maturidi shows why this is impossible from both a linguistic and rational perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi first establishes the agreed linguistic meaning of irja'.
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
Find the two verses he cites. Reflect on how they support the meaning of postponement.
Verses: 26:36 and 9:106.
3
Understand the Hashwiyya claim
What did they say about the Murji'ah's naming? Write it in your own words.
They claimed the name came from not counting all good deeds as faith.
4
Follow Maturidi's refutation
Why does he say this claim conflicts with language? Why also with reason?
Consider the principle that every good deed has its own specific name.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Ask yourself: what is the correct linguistic derivation of the name Murji'ah?
The answer is postponement, not a theological stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: في الإرجاء. ثم اختلف في المعنى الذي سمّى به من سمّي مرجئا، بعد اتفاق أهل اللسان على أن الإرجاء هو التأخير؛ وعلى ذلك قوله: {أرجه وأخاه}، وقال: {مرجون لأمر الله}. قالت الحشوية: سميت المرجئة بما لم يُسمّوا كل الخيرات إيمانا. وهذا مما لا يحتمله اللسان ولا العقل. فأما اللسان فهو أن الإرجاء هو التأخير، ولا وجه لهذا الاسم فيما يسمى كل خير باسمه الخاص، ومنع هذا الاسم العام.
Question: Concerning Irja'. Then they differed about the meaning by which those called Murji'ah were named, after the people of language agreed that irja' is postponement; and upon that is His saying: {Postpone him and his brother}, and He said: {Those postponed for the command of God}. The Hashwiyya said: The Murji'ah were named because they did not count all good deeds as faith. This is something that neither language nor reason can bear. As for language, irja' is postponement, and there is no justification for this name concerning that which calls every good by its specific name, and prevents this general name.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological refutation of the Hashwiyya's claim regarding the naming of the Murji'ah. Maturidi begins by stating that there is agreement among linguists (ahl al-lisan) that the word irja' means postponement (ta'khir). He provides Quranic evidence: {Postpone him and his brother} (Surah al-Shu'ara' 36) and {Those postponed for the command of God} (Surah al-Tawba 106). Then he presents the Hashwiyya's claim: that the Murji'ah were so named because they did not consider all good deeds as part of faith (iman). Maturidi rejects this, saying it is linguistically and rationally untenable. He explains: since irja' linguistically means postponement, there is no connection between this meaning and the Hashwiyya's claim. The name 'Murji'ah' must be derived from the root meaning of irja', not from a theological stance about counting good deeds. Maturidi points out that every good deed has its own specific name (e.g., prayer, charity), and calling them all by the general name 'iman' would be a misuse. The Hashwiyya's interpretation is therefore a distortion that language does not support and reason cannot accept.

Key Terms
إرجاء (irja')
مرجئة (Murji'ah)
حشوية (Hashwiyya)
تأخير (ta'khir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'ah were named because they excluded deeds from faith (i.e., they did not consider all good deeds as part of iman).
Correction: Maturidi shows that irja' means postponement linguistically, and the name Murji'ah derives from that meaning, as seen in Quranic usage. The theological stance about deeds is not the basis for the name.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main linguistic argument against the Hashwiyya's claim about the naming of the Murji'ah?
A) The word irja' means 'counting good deeds', so the Hashwiyya are correct.
B) The word irja' means 'postponement', and the Hashwiyya's explanation does not match this linguistic meaning.
C) The Hashwiyya are wrong because they themselves are Murji'ah.
D) The Quran does not use the word irja'.

Key Takeaway
The name 'Murji'ah' is rooted in the linguistic meaning of 'postponement' (irja'), not in any theological stance on faith and deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged a group or person by a label without understanding its true meaning? How does Maturidi's emphasis on linguistic accuracy challenge you to be more precise in your own judgments, especially about those you disagree with? Will you commit to investigating the origins of labels before using them?
?
When you hear the term 'Murji'ah', do you immediately associate it with a negative theological stance? This lesson shows the name originates from a neutral Quranic meaning. How does this affect your perception of historical groups? Are there labels you apply to others today that might be similarly misunderstood? What steps will you take to ensure you don't misrepresent others based on mistaken etymology?
?
Maturidi argues that the Hashwiyya's claim is linguistically impossible, and that our beliefs must align with clear language and reason. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that contradict clear linguistic or rational evidence? How willing are you to re-examine them in light of clear proof, even if it means changing your position? Will you set aside pride and seek the truth, no matter where it leads?

The Meaning of Irja' and the Naming of the Murji'a
Maturidi defines irja' and refutes both Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila on the Murji'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila regarding the naming of the Murji'a, and the proper linguistic and rational meaning of irja'.

Core Idea
Irja' means postponement, not exclusion of good deeds from faith.

This passage tackles a key sectarian dispute: why are the Murji'a called by that name? Maturidi begins by affirming the linguistic consensus that irja' means postponement, then systematically dismantles the Hashwiyya claim that the name derives from not calling all good deeds iman. He also addresses the Mu'tazila definition, agreeing partially but distinguishing the blameworthy group. The lesson will walk through these arguments, showing how Maturidi grounds theology in language and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall flow and key terms. Notice that Maturidi cites Quranic verses and then engages two opposing groups.
Pay attention to the terms: الإرجاء, الحشوية, المعتزلة.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Locate where Maturidi introduces the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila positions. What does each group claim about the meaning of irja' and the naming of the Murji'a?
The Hashwiyya claim the Murji'a are named for not calling good deeds iman; the Mu'tazila say they postponed judgment on major sins.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
Maturidi first argues linguistically against the Hashwiyya. He uses a disjunction (either 'iman' is truly a name for every good deed or not) to show the inconsistency. Trace his reasoning step by step.
The key point: if iman is a name for each good deed, then no one would refuse to call them iman; if not, then calling someone by a false name is unjust.
4
Examine the rational argument
Maturidi then argues intellectually: the mind perceives realities through senses or reflection, and neither supports the Hashwiyya's claim. Instead, the real meaning is that the Murji'a postponed their own faith and made exceptions.
This shifts the definition from actions to an internal state of postponement.
5
Review the Mu'tazila and the Abu Hanifa anecdote
Maturidi agrees with the Mu'tazila that irja' involves postponing judgment on major sins, but insists the blameworthy ones are not simply those who hold that view. The anecdote from Abu Hanifa connects irja' to the angels' response in the Quran.
The anecdote is incomplete but suggests a deeper theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ في الإرجاء ] ثم اختلف في المعنى الذي سمّى به من سمّى مُرجنّا، بعد اتفاق أهل اللسان على الإرجاء أنه التأخير؛ وعلى ذلك قوله: أرجه وأخاه، وقال: مُرجَون لأمر الله. قالت الحشوية: سميت المرجئة بما لم يُسمّوا كل الخيرات إيمانا. وهذا مما لا يحتمله اللسان ولا العقل. فأما اللسان فهو أن الإرجاء هو التأخير، ولا وجه لهذا الاسم فيما يسمى كل خير باسمه الخاص، ومنع هذا الاسم العام. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون هذا في الحقيقة اسما لكل أو لا. فإن كان اسما له فمن يأبى تسمية الشيء باسمه الذي هو اسمه في الحقيقة جهلاً به أو تعنتا؟ فلا أحد يسميه بهذا الاسم، فما بال هؤلاء سموا به خصوصا من بين جميع الخلق؟ ولو كان بذا يلزم هؤلاء هذا الاسم فهو لازم لمن سماهم به لأنهم وقت التسمية بهذا تاركون الأسماء الخاصة لها، فيصيرون بذلك مستحقين لهذا الاسم. ثم بقولهم: الإيمان اسم لاجتماع الخيرات إبطال هذا الاسم عن كل خير على الانفراد، فيلزمهم هذا. أو ليس باسم لها في الحقيقة، فلا وجه لتسمية من سمّي الشيء بما ليس ذلك باسم له، ويكون ذلك في الحقيقة سمة الصادقين بالاسم المذموم عنده في الدين، فقد أعلى درجة الكاذبين عند الله وحط درجة الصادقين، وذلك عظيم عند من يعقل. وأما العقل فإنها تدرك حقائق الأشياء بجهتين إما بما تؤدي المشاعر المجعولة مسلكاً؟ وهي الحواس، أو بالتدبر في علم الحس وما أظهر الدليل وليس في شيء من المحسوس إيجاب ذلك، ولا كان فيه مما يستخرج بالتأمل: حقيقة الإرجاء أنه فيمن لا يسمّي الخيرات إيمانا - ولا قوة إلا بالله؛ بل ذلك في الحقيقة مذهبهم حين أرجوا دينهم ولم يشهدوا [ بالإيمان] لأنفسهم واستثنوا في ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقالت المعتزلة: المرجئة هي التي أرجت الكبائر، لم تنزل أهلها نارا ولا جنة. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: هذا الذي قالوه حق في لزوم إرجاء تلك الأعمال؛ لكن المروى بالذم ليسوا هم إن ثبت خبر الدم. وهذا هو الحق. وعن مثله سئل أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: الدم. أخذت الإرجاء؟ فقال: من فعل الملائكة، حيث قيل لهم أنبؤني بأسماء هؤلاء إن كنتم.
Issue [concerning al-Irja']. Then they differed over the meaning by which the one who was named 'Murji'' was named, after the people of language agreed that al-irja' is postponement; and upon that is His saying: 'Put him off and his brother' and He said: 'and others deferred (murjawna) for the command of God.' The Hashwiyya said: The Murji'a were named because they do not call all good deeds iman. This is something that neither language nor intellect can bear. As for language, it is that al-irja' is postponement, and there is no aspect for this name in that which every good deed is called by its specific name, and this general name is prevented. Then it is not free from either that this is in reality a name for every [good deed] or not. If it is a name for it, then who would refuse to name a thing by its name which is its name in reality out of ignorance or obstinacy? No one names it by this name, so why are these [Murji'a] specifically named with this name among all creation? And if by this this name is necessitated for these, then it is necessary for those who named them because at the time of naming they are leaving the specific names for them, thus becoming deserving of this name. Then by their saying: iman is a name for the totality of good deeds, they invalidate this name for every good deed individually, so this is necessitated for them. Or it is not in reality a name for them, so there is no basis for naming one who names a thing by what is not its name, and that would in reality be a mark of the truthful by the blameworthy name in religion, so it elevates the degree of the liars with God and lowers the degree of the truthful, and that is grave for those who understand. As for the intellect, it perceives the realities of things in two ways: either by what the senses made as a path convey – and they are the faculties – or by reflection in the science of calculation and what evidence shows. And there is nothing in the sensory that necessitates that, nor is there in what is extracted through contemplation: the reality of al-irja' is that it is concerning those who do not call good deeds iman – and there is no power except with God; rather that is in reality their school when they postponed their religion and did not testify [to faith] for themselves and made exceptions in that. And there is no power except with God. And the Mu'tazila said: The Murji'a are those who postponed the major sins, not assigning their people to the Fire or Paradise. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: This which they said is true concerning the necessity of postponing those deeds; but those who are blamed (al-marwī bi-al-dhamm) are not them, if the report of blame is established. And this is the truth. And about the like of which Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him) was asked: 'the blood? Did you adopt al-irja'?' He said: 'From the action of the angels, when it was said to them: Inform me of the names of these if you are...'
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion about the name 'Murji'a' and its proper meaning. It begins by stating the issue of al-irja' (postponement) and noting that linguists agree it means 'postponement', citing Quranic verses. Then the Hashwiyya claim that the Murji'a are so named because they do not call all good deeds 'iman' (faith). Maturidi rejects this as linguistically and rationally untenable. Linguistically, he argues that irja' means postponement, so there is no connection to naming good deeds. He then presents a disjunction: either 'iman' is truly a name for every good deed or not. If it is, then no one would refuse to name a good deed as iman, so why single out the Murji'a? If not, then calling someone by a name that is not theirs is unjust, as it would falsely label the truthful with a blameworthy name. Rationally, the intellect perceives realities through senses or reflection; neither supports the claim that irja' is about not calling good deeds iman. Instead, the true meaning is that the Murji'a postponed their religion and did not affirm faith for themselves without exceptions. Then Maturidi addresses the Mu'tazila, who define the Murji'a as those who postpone judgment on major sins. He agrees that postponing the judgment of deeds is accurate, but argues that those blamed in traditions are not the Mu'tazila's referent—provided the report of blame is authentic. He concludes by mentioning an anecdote where Abu Hanifa was asked about adopting irja' and replied that it is from the action of the angels (referring to when they were asked to inform of names). The anecdote is incomplete in the text.

Key Terms
الإرجاء
المرجئة
الحشوية
المعتزلة
الإيمان
التكذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what is the linguistic meaning of al-irja'?
A) Excluding good deeds from faith
B) Postponement
C) Doubt about faith
D) Denial of major sins

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the name 'Murji'a' comes from postponement of judgment, not from denying good deeds as faith.

Maturidi's Critique of Mu'tazila on Irja' of Naming the Sinner
Maturidi criticizes Mu'tazila's irja' and contrasts it with correct irja'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for their irja' regarding the naming of the sinner and how this differs from the correct irja' concerning God's ultimate judgment.

Core Idea
Not all irja' is equal: ignorance of human action is inexcusable; ignorance of God's decree is necessary.

Maturidi here engages in a polemic against the Mu'tazila, who refused to call the major sinner either a believer or an unbeliever. He argues that their suspension of judgment stems from ignorance of the true nature of the act, which is inexcusable. In contrast, the proper irja' concerns God's ultimate judgment, which is known only through revelation. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the two occurrences of 'irja' and how they are contrasted.
2
Identify the two forms of irja'
Find the irja' attributed to the Mu'tazila and the 'correct' irja' that Maturidi implies is necessary. Note what each is based on.
One irja' is based on ignorance of the action; the other on ignorance of God's decree.
3
Understand the critique
Ask: Why is the Mu'tazila's irja' blameworthy? Why is the other irja' necessary? The commentary will help clarify.
The key difference is the object of ignorance: human action vs. divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة أرجت فعل نفسه حيث أبي تسميته مؤمناً وكافرا، فجهله بحقيقته ألزمه القول بإرجاء الاسم. لكنه جهل حقيقة فعله فلا عذر له. والأول جهل حقيقة ما يعمل به الله ، وذلك لا يعرف إلا بالسمع، ولم يجئ ما يقطع القول بشيء، فهو لازم.
And the Mu'tazila postponed the action itself when they refused to call him a believer or an unbeliever, so their ignorance of its reality necessitated that they affirm irja' of the name. But he is ignorant of the reality of his action, so he has no excuse. And the first (the correct irja') is ignorance of the reality of what God does with him, and that is not known except through revelation, and nothing has come that decisively states anything, so it is necessary.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage contrasts two forms of irja'. Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for their irja' regarding the naming of the major sinner: they refuse to call him either a believer or an unbeliever, thus suspending judgment. This irja' is based on their ignorance of the true reality of the sinner's action (whether it constitutes unbelief or not). Maturidi argues that such ignorance is inexcusable because the reality of the action can be known. In contrast, the correct irja' (the one defended earlier) is based on ignorance of what God will ultimately do with the person—whether He will forgive or punish—which is known only through revelation and for which no definitive text exists. Therefore, the correct irja' is necessary, while the Mu'tazila's irja' is blameworthy.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الإرجاء
فعل النفس
الاسم
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That irja' is always a Murji'ite position and therefore blameworthy.
Correction: Maturidi shows that there is a necessary irja' concerning God's decree, which is distinct from the blameworthy irja' of the Mu'tazila regarding naming the sinner.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi criticize the Mu'tazila's irja'?
A) Because it denies divine forgiveness.
B) Because it is based on ignorance of the reality of the sinner's action, which is inexcusable.
C) Because it follows the Murji'a.
D) Because it affirms eternal punishment.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi distinguishes between an inexcusable irja' based on ignorance of human actions and a necessary irja' based on ignorance of God's unseen decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Your life is full of unknowns about God's plan. Are you truly comfortable with that necessary ignorance, or do you secretly demand certainty about your fate? How can you practice the praiseworthy irja' today?

Refuting Irja' on Ali's Conflict
Maturidi refutes irja' over Ali's conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Murji'a applying irja' to the conflict between Ali and his opponents, and why he considers ignorance of Ali's merit inexcusable.

Core Idea
Ali's merit is clear; irja' becomes blameworthy when it masks the truth.

Maturidi addresses a specific claim by some who define Murji'a as those who suspend judgment on Ali and his opponents. He unpacks two possible meanings of irja' and argues that if it means blameworthy postponement, it is still close to correct because Ali's merit is undeniable. He then provides evidence from hadith and consensus to show that anyone who doubts Ali's right is guilty of inexcusable ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the opening sentence where some define Murji'a as those who postpone Ali's matter. Notice that Maturidi immediately analyzes the term irja'.
The claim has two possible meanings of irja'.
2
Examine the evidence
Identify the hadith about Abu Bakr, Umar, and Ali, and how Umar included Ali in the shura, and the agreement of the Companions. See how Maturidi builds his case.
The hadith is from the time of Abu Bakr.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the matter of Ali was not obscure. Reflect on why he considers ignorance of this to be inexcusable.
Ignorance here is due to negligence or lack of reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم: المرجئة هم الذين أرجوا أمر علي بن أبي طالب ومن خرج معه وعليه فإن أرادوا به «الإرجاء» من الوقف في القول فيهم فلا معنى لذلك من غيره. وإن أرادوا الإرجاء المذموم فهو قريب لما لم يكن أحد يعدل عليا في الاستحقاق؛ مع دلالة الخبر المرفوع له في عهد أبي بكر رضي الله عنه أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «إن وليتم أبا بكر تجدونه ضعيفا في بدنه قويا في دينه، وإن وليتم عمر وجدتموه قويًا في بدنه قويًا في دينه، وإن وليتم عليا وجدتموه هاديًا مهديًا يسلك بكم طريق الهدى» ، أو كما قال عليه السلام؛ ثم إدخال عمر إياه في الشورى، ثم اتفاق أخيار الصحابة عليه؛ لم يكن أمره بحيث الخفاء ليُعذر من جوز القول: جائز أن يلحق أهله الذم بذلك، إذ هو جهل ما لا يحتمل الجهل إلا عن إغفال أو ترك التأمل في أمر الدين. والله الموفق.
And some said: The Murji'a are those who postponed the matter of Ali ibn Abi Talib and those who went out with him and against him. If they intended by 'irja'' the suspension of speech concerning them, then there is no meaning to that apart from it. And if they intended the blameworthy irja', then it is close to being correct, since no one was equal to Ali in merit; with the evidence of the raised hadith concerning him in the time of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, that the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'If you appoint Abu Bakr, you will find him weak in body but strong in religion; if you appoint Umar, you will find him strong in body and strong in religion; if you appoint Ali, you will find him a guide, rightly guided, leading you on the path of guidance,' or as he said, peace be upon him; then Umar's including him in the shura, then the agreement of the righteous Companions upon him; his matter was not so obscure that one who allowed the saying that it is permissible that his people be blamed for that could be excused, for it is ignorance that does not admit excuse except through negligence or leaving reflection on the matter of religion. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage addresses a claim that some Murji'a applied irja' to the historical conflict between Ali and his opponents (e.g., Mu'awiya). Maturidi argues that if they meant irja' in the sense of suspending judgment about who was right, that is meaningless because Ali's merit is clearly established. He cites a hadith from the time of Abu Bakr that praises Ali, and notes that Umar included Ali in the shura council, and the virtuous Companions agreed on him. Therefore, his claim to the caliphate was not obscure. Anyone who suggests that it is possible that Ali's followers deserve blame is guilty of inexcusable ignorance. This is a refutation of those who use irja' to avoid taking a position on the conflict, implying that the truth about Ali's right is clear.

Key Terms
المرجئة
علي بن أبي طالب
الإرجاء
الخبر المرفوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think irja' is always neutral, but Maturidi shows it can be blameworthy when applied to clear historical facts.
Correction: Irja' is only acceptable when the truth is genuinely unknown; here Ali's merit is clear, so suspending judgment becomes blameworthy.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi dismiss the irja' regarding Ali and his opponents?
A) Because the hadith condemns irja'.
B) Because Ali's merit and rightness are clearly established, so ignorance is inexcusable.
C) Because the Mu'tazila also reject it.
D) Because it leads to eternal punishment.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that Ali's merit is so clear from hadith and consensus that any suspension of judgment (irja') is blameworthy and inexcusable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you suspend judgment to avoid confronting the truth, even when clear evidence is before you? How does Maturidi's refutation challenge your tendency to remain neutral when clarity demands action? Will you examine one such area today and commit to acknowledging the truth?
?
When you encounter clear evidence about a person's merit or a matter of faith, do you acknowledge it openly or withhold judgment to avoid conflict with others? How does this lesson expose your own form of 'blameworthy irja'? What step can you take to align your speech with the truth?
?
Maturidi says ignorance is inexcusable when evidence is clear. Is there a truth about your own faith or practice that you have been neglecting to examine? What will you do about it today, starting with one concrete action?

Definition of Murji'ah and the Clear Merit of Ali
Maturidi defines the Murji'ah and refutes a specific opinion about Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of the Murji'ah, the two senses of irja', and why ignorance about Ali's merit is inexcusable in his view.

Core Idea
True ignorance about Ali's status reflects neglect of clear proofs.

Maturidi begins by clarifying who the Murji'ah are and what their 'irja'' means. He then argues that any doubt about Ali's merit is inexcusable because of explicit prophetic testimony and consensus among the companions. This passage shows how theological definitions have direct implications for evaluating early Islamic conflicts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two meanings of irja'
Read the passage carefully to see how Maturidi distinguishes between permissible suspension of judgment (waqf) and blameworthy irja'. This distinction is key to understanding his critique.
Notice that the word 'irja'' can mean either suspension or a blameworthy postponement of faith.
2
Identify the proofs for Ali's merit
Maturidi cites three proofs: a hadith praising Ali, Umar's inclusion of Ali in the shura, and the consensus of the righteous companions. List each one and see how they build the case.
The hadith explicitly calls Ali a 'guide' and 'guided one'.
3
Grasp why ignorance is blameworthy
Reflect on Maturidi's conclusion: such clear evidence means that anyone who doubts Ali's merit is guilty of negligence or abandoning reflection. Ask yourself what this says about the responsibility to seek knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم: المرجئة هم الذين أرجوا أمر علي بن أبي طالب ومن خرج معه وعليه فإن أرادوا به «الإرجاء» من الوقف في القول فيهم فلا معنى لذلك من غيره. وإن أرادوا الإرجاء المذموم فهو قريب لما لم يكن أحد يعدل عليا في الاستحقاق؛ مع دلالة الخبر المرفوع له في عهد أبي بكر رضي الله عنه أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «إن وليتم أبا بكر تجدونه ضعيفا في بدنه قويا في دينه، وإن وليتم عمر وجدتموه قويًا في بدنه قويًا في دينه، وإن وليتم عليا وجدتموه هاديًا مهديًا يسلك بكم طريق الهدى» ، أو كما قال عليه السلام؛ ثم إدخال عمر إياه في الشورى، ثم اتفاق أخيار الصحابة عليه؛ لم يكن أمره بحيث الخفاء ليُعذر من جوز القول: جائز أن يلحق أهله الذم بذلك، إذ هو جهل ما لا يحتمل الجهل إلا عن إغفال أو ترك التأمل في أمر الدين. والله الموفق.
And some said: The Murji'ah are those who postponed the matter of Ali ibn Abi Talib and those who went out with him and against him. If they intended by it 'al-irja'' (postponement) meaning suspension of judgment concerning them, then there is no meaning to that from other than it. And if they intended the blameworthy irja', then it is close, because there was no one who equated Ali in merit; with the indication of the marfu' hadith concerning him in the era of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'If you appoint Abu Bakr, you will find him weak in body, strong in his religion; if you appoint Umar, you will find him strong in body, strong in his religion; if you appoint Ali, you will find him a guide, guided, leading you on the path of guidance' — or as he, peace be upon him, said; then Umar's inclusion of him in the shura, then the agreement of the righteous companions upon him; his affair was not such as to be hidden so that one who permitted the statement 'it is possible that blame should attach to its people' could be excused, for it is ignorance of what cannot be excused except through negligence or abandoning reflection on the matter of religion. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage defines the Murji'ah and refutes a specific opinion about Ali. Maturidi begins by citing a view that the Murji'ah are those who postponed judgment on the conflict between Ali and his opponents. He then distinguishes two meanings of irja': (1) mere suspension of judgment, which is meaningless here, and (2) blameworthy irja', which is close to the truth because Ali's merit was so clear. To demonstrate this clarity, he cites a hadith in which the Prophet foretold the qualities of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Ali, showing Ali's status as a guide. Further, Umar included Ali in the shura council, and the righteous companions agreed upon him. Thus, ignorance of Ali's merit is inexcusable—it can only stem from negligence or failure to reflect on religion. Maturidi concludes that those who consider blame possible are guilty of inexcusable ignorance.

Key Terms

الإرجاء
الوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that all who suspend judgment on early Islamic conflicts are Murji'ah.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between permissible suspension (waqf) and blameworthy irja'. The latter is only blameworthy when the truth is clear, as in the case of Ali's merit.

Checkpoint

A) Because Ali was clearly the most deserving of all companions
B) Because of the explicit hadith about his qualities and the consensus of the companions
C) Because it is a matter of doctrine that requires immediate judgment
D) Because the Murji'ah themselves admitted his merit

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's twofold categorization of irja' shows that doctrinal ambiguity about Ali's merit is not a neutral position but a failure to engage with clear evidence.

?
The righteous companions agreed on Ali's merit. Is your heart in harmony with them, or have you allowed sectarian loyalties, gossip, or personal bias to create doubt? Will you repent from that today and align your heart with the Salaf?

The Second Meaning of Ijja' and the Three Categories of People
Maturidi defines the second meaning of Ijja' and divides people into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi defines the second meaning of Ijja' (suspension of judgment) and how he classifies people into believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites based on their inner and outer states.

Core Idea
Ijja' is not just one concept—it takes different forms in different sects, and the hypocrite occupies a unique third category.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi continues his discussion of Ijja' by exploring a second meaning: suspending judgment about one's own faith. He then reviews the positions of the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila, and concludes with a threefold classification of humanity: believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites. This classification highlights the difference between outward appearance and inner reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second meaning of Ijja'
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi explains that Ijja' can refer to the agent's suspension of judgment about his own action. The Hashwiyya apply this by not definitively claiming belief but making exceptions.
Note that Ijja' here is about one's own state, not about judging others.
2
Examine the views of the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila
The Hashwiyya suspend judgment on their own faith by saying 'if God wills,' while the Mu'tazila suspend judgment on the status of a committer of a major sin, refusing to call him believer or disbeliever.
These are two different applications of the same concept.
3
Learn the three categories of people
Maturidi states that God divided people into believers and disbelievers in truth, and made a third category: the hypocrite. The hypocrite appears with believers outwardly but is inwardly with disbelievers, receiving different rulings in this world and the Hereafter.
This classification is based on both inner conviction and outer action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني أن يكون ذلك فيها عليه حال الفاعل في فعله من الوقف في ذلك، نحو ما قالت الحشوية في اسم المؤمن والدُّنْيا فيه . ومعلوم أن الإرجاء هو الوقف في الجواب والإمهال للنظر . ثم لا يقطعون في أنفسهم القول بالإيمان بل يستثنون، والدُّنْيا إرجاء، وقد ذكر ذلك في بعض الأخبار، لكن لا يشهد بصحته. وفي العقل بيان معنى الإرجاء، إذ هو الوقف في الأمر في أمر هو فعلهم. وما قالت المعتزلة في إرجاء صاحب الكبيرة بالتسمية أنه مؤمن أو كافر. مع ما قسم الخلق الذين امتحنوا قسمين في التحقيق مؤمن وكافر، وصيّر القسم الثالث المنافق؛ إذ هو مع هؤلاء في الظاهر، ومع هؤلاء في السر، فاستوجب أحكام أهل الإيمان في الظاهر مما عليه أهل الأديان في الدنيا، واستوجب في الباطن الأحكام مما عليه أمر الكفر في الظاهر، من أمر الآخرة. والله الموفق.
And the second aspect is that this [suspension] applies to the agent's state regarding his action in suspending judgment about it, like what the Hashwiyya said concerning the name of the believer and worldly affairs therein. It is known that Ijja' is the suspension of judgment and granting respite for consideration. Then they do not definitively assert belief in themselves but they make exceptions, and worldly affairs are [a form of] Ijja'. This has been mentioned in some reports, but nothing testifies to its authenticity. In reason, the meaning of Ijja' is clear, for it is the suspension of judgment in a matter that is their action. And the Mu'tazila said concerning the Ijja' of the committer of a major sin, by refusing to name him a believer or a disbeliever. Along with this, He divided the tested people into two categories in truth: believer and disbeliever, and made the third category the hypocrite; for he is with these outwardly and with these secretly, so he deserves the rulings of the people of faith outwardly, according to what the people of religions [follow] in this world, and inwardly he deserves the rulings according to the matter of disbelief outwardly, concerning the matters of the Hereafter. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the second meaning of Ijja' (suspension of judgment) and its application to different sects. Maturidi begins by explaining that the second aspect of Ijja' involves the agent's own state regarding his action—suspending judgment about it, as the Hashwiyya do when they call someone a believer but still make exceptions (istithna') regarding worldly matters. He notes that Ijja' is defined as suspending judgment and granting time for reflection, but the Hashwiyya do not definitively affirm belief; they always say 'if God wills.' Then Maturidi clarifies that in rational terms, Ijja' is suspension concerning an action that is one's own deed. He then addresses the Mu'tazila, who practice a form of Ijja' regarding the committer of a major sin by refusing to call him either believer or disbeliever. Maturidi then presents a classification: God divided people into two categories in truth—believers and disbelievers—but made a third category: the hypocrite (munafiq). The hypocrite appears outwardly with believers but inwardly is with disbelievers. Thus, in this world, the hypocrite receives the external rulings of believers (like marriage, inheritance, burial), but in the hereafter he receives the punishment of disbelievers. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms

حشوية
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ijja' is a single, uniform concept with only one meaning.
Correction: Maturidi shows that Ijja' has multiple applications: the Hashwiyya apply it to their own faith, the Mu'tazila apply it to the status of major sinners, and it can also refer to suspending judgment in general.

Key Takeaway
The second meaning of Ijja' is suspending judgment about one's own faith, and humanity is divided into three categories: believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites, with the hypocrite receiving different rulings in this world and the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God divided people into only three categories, and the hypocrite is the most dangerous because he deceives himself and others, where do you truly stand? Are your secrets consistent with your public profession of faith?

The Error of Making Exceptions in Faith
Maturidi condemns the use of 'except' in faith and critiques three sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers using 'except' in faith as ingratitude and how it relates to the views of the Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hashwiyya.

Core Idea
Using 'except' in faith is not humility but ingratitude.

Maturidi argues that making exceptions in declaring faith reveals a hidden attitude of ingratitude towards Allah's favors. He examines the reasoning behind this practice and refutes the positions of three theological groups who permit it. In this passage, he lays out the logical consequences and warns against the danger of doubt concealed in the word 'except'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the sin of istithna in faith
Maturidi begins by highlighting Allah's favors in guiding to faith. Read the opening verses and see how he sets the stage for condemning exceptions.
2
Follow the three scenarios
The author outlines three states of one who makes exceptions: knowing sincerity, not knowing, or knowing insincerity. Each scenario leads to ruin or ingratitude.
Notice that even if one knows sincerity, using 'except' conceals Allah's favors.
3
Grasp the principle of al-thunya
Maturidi explains that 'thunya' is used for caution, but in faith it cannot save one from wrath.
Caution in faith is actually a form of ingratitude.
4
Apply to the sects
See how the Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hashwiyya are criticized for their use of istithna. For the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, it shows perpetual ignorance; for the Hashwiyya, it is inconsistent with their own principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنعمت عليهم وبالامتنان بقوله بل الله يمن عليكم الآية وبالتزيين في القلوب والتحبيب بقوله ولكن الله حبب إليكم الإيمان وزينه في قلوبكم وبالإفضال بقوله ولولا فضل الله عليكم ورحمته الآية فلا يخلو من يستثنى من أن يكون عرف صدق نفسه وعظيم نعم الله وإفضاله أو لم يعلم ذلك أو علم أنه على غير ذلك فإن علم أنه على غير ذلك فبعدا له فإن الدنيا لا تنفعه سوى الارتياب فيما زعم أنه لم يعلمه وإن لم يعلم صدقه فيما قال ولا امتنان الله وإنعامه فويل له إذ جهل أعظم نعم الله وكفر به وإن علم ذلك فإن في حرف الشك عند السامعين ستر نعم الله وكفران مننه فذلك آية الزوال وسبب المحق والله الموفق ثم الأصل عندنا أن الثنيا حرف يستعمل في موضع التحرج وهذا موضع لو تحقق الذي له يتحرج لا ينفعه التحرج بل يلزمه مقت الله ونقمته ولو لم يتحقق يلحقه حكم كفران نعم الله حيث لم يره منه ولم يشكر له إذ أوجب له ولايته وأضاف إلى نفسه الإخراج من الظلمات إلى النور ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم الحق على مذهب المعتزلة والخوارج والحشوية الاستثناء في الدين وبخاصة في الإيمان فأما عند المعتزلة والخوارج فإنه يخرج من حيث لا يشعر به ويمتنع عن الإجابة من حيث لا يعلم به وإذا كان كذلك فهو أبدا في جهل من حاله فحقه أن لا يتسمى به وعلى ذلك لم يسمع أحد سمى نفسه برا تقيا زكيا طيبا مطيعا الله إذ هو اسم لأحد نوعي الخيرات أو لهما جميعا فالإيمان عند ذلك ما كان لهم التسمى به دون الدنيا وكذلك الحشوية إذ القول عندهم في الإيمان وفي كل من أسماء المدح واحد ولا يسمون بغير ذلك بلا تنيا وفي لزوم هؤلاء في مذهبهم الثنيا ولا قوة إلا بالله
He favored them, and by благодарность (imtinan) in His saying: 'But Allah bestows favor upon you' [the verse], and by beautification in hearts and making beloved in His saying: 'But Allah has made faith beloved to you and has beautified it in your hearts' [49:7], and by grace in His saying: 'And if not for the favor of Allah upon you and His mercy' [the verse]. So one who makes an exception cannot be but that he knows the truthfulness of himself and the greatness of Allah's favors and grace, or does not know that, or knows that he is other than that. If he knows that he is other than that, then away with him, for the world does not benefit him except doubt in what he claims he did not know. And if he does not know the truthfulness of what he said nor Allah's blessings and favors, then woe to him, for he is ignorant of the greatest favors of Allah and disbelieves in them. And if he knows that, then the letter of doubt in the hearing of the listeners is a concealment of Allah's favors and ingratitude for His blessings, so that is a sign of removal and a cause of destruction. And Allah grants success. Then the principle with us is that 'al-thunya' (the word of exception) is used in a place of caution. And this is a place where if the one who is cautious is certain, caution does not benefit him; rather, the wrath of Allah and His punishment befall him. And if he is not certain, then the ruling of ingratitude for Allah's favors applies to him, since he did not see them from Him and did not thank Him, for he obligated His guardianship for him and attributed to himself the bringing out from darkness to light. And there is no power except with Allah. Then the truth according to the Madhhab of the Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hashwiyya is the use of exception (istithna) in religion, especially in faith. As for the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, one may exit (faith) without perceiving it, and refrain from answering without knowing it. And if that is the case, he is perpetually ignorant of his state, so it is his right not to be named with it. And upon that, no one has ever called himself pious, righteous, pure, good, obedient to Allah, because it is a name for one of the two types of good deeds or for both of them. So faith in that regard is what they cannot name themselves with except without (exception) in this world. And similarly the Hashwiyya, for the statement with them concerning faith and concerning all names of praise is one, and they do not name themselves with other than that without exception, and it is necessary for these people in their madhhab to use exception. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage condemns the practice of making exceptions (istithna) in declaring one's faith, arguing that it implies ingratitude and leads to ruin. The author begins by stating that one who uses 'except' (illa) in faith either knows his own sincerity and God's blessings or does not. If he knows but still uses the exception, it is a concealment of God's favors and a cause of removal. If he does not know, he is ignorant of God's greatest blessings. The principle is then stated that the word of exception (al-thunya) is used in situations of caution, but here caution does not benefit; it incurs God's wrath. Then the author applies this to the Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Hashwiyya, who permit istithna in faith. For the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, they are always in ignorance of their state and thus should not even claim the name of faith. The Hashwiyya similarly require exception for all praiseworthy names. The passage concludes that no one has ever called themselves pious without exception, because faith is a name that cannot be claimed without certainty. The author emphasizes that using exception in faith is always wrong and contrary to the attitude of gratitude.

الثنيا (al-thunya)
الإيمان
المعتزلة

الحشوية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using 'except' in faith shows humility and caution.
Correction: Maturidi shows it is actually a concealment of Allah's favors and ingratitude, leading to ruin.

Checkpoint

A) He is excused because of caution.
B) It is a sign of ingratitude and leads to removal of blessings.
C) It shows humility and is acceptable.
D) It is permissible only for the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
Using 'except' in faith is a form of ingratitude that contradicts the recognition of Allah's favors and leads to ruin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even silently, added an 'if Allah wills' to your certainty that you are a believer? This lesson says that such an exception is not humility but ingratitude. Are you willing to remove that doubt and acknowledge Allah's favor completely? What would that change in your daily life?
?
The lesson says that if you know the truth of your faith and still use 'except', you are concealing Allah's favor. Have you ever downplayed the gift of iman in your speech, perhaps to appear modest or for fear of hypocrisy? How can you change your speech to express gratitude instead?
?
Maturidi states that one who makes an exception is either ignorant or insincere. Which category do you fall into? If you are not sure, then you are in the category of ignorance. What step will you take today to move from ignorance to certainty about your iman?

Refuting the Scriptural Justification for Istithna in Faith
Maturidi answers the objection that the Qur'an allows istithna, showing it does not apply to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the argument that Qur'anic instances of istithna (exception) justify its use in declarations of faith.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's use of 'if Allah wills' does not imply uncertainty in faith.

Maturidi now addresses a key objection: doesn't the Qur'an itself use exceptions like 'if Allah wills'? If God uses such language, why can't we? This passage masterfully turns the tables, showing that the objector's own assumptions defeat the argument. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a statement, then an objection, then a multi-part refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objector's claim
Find the part where an objector says: 'Allah mentioned the world in places of doubt, so exception is permissible.' Also the verse about entering the mosque. Write down the objection in your own words.
The objector uses Qur'anic examples to argue that istithna is allowed.
3
Follow Maturidi's first refutation
Maturidi responds that the objector's own madhhab already establishes doubt as a principle, so the argument does not apply. Also, Allah addresses believers with certainty in many verses; if exception were allowed, those verses would be meaningless.
This shows inconsistency in the objector's position.
4
Follow Maturidi's second refutation
Maturidi points out that Allah uses words like 'zann' (conjecture) and 'la'alla' (perhaps) in contexts of certainty. If the objector were consistent, he would use those words when asked about his faith, but he doesn't.
This undermines the objector's analogy.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on how Maturidi dismantles the objection by turning the objector's own tools against him. The key takeaway: istithna in faith would contradict the definitive nature of belief and the Qur'an's own treatment of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الله تعالى قال يا أيها الذين آمنوا في غير موضع باسم مقطوع لم يجز أن يستحق شيئا مما جرى الخطاب به من أمر ونهي ووعد ووعيد وترغيب وترهيب فيكون عامة آيات الله في الخطاب خارجة مخرج عبث إذ الحق من جملة المذاهب من لا يلزمه هذا القول قالته أو لم يقلله والله الموفق فإن قال قائل فقد ذكر الله الدنيا في غير موضع الشك فيجوز الثنيا على ذلك ثم قوله لتدخلن المسجد الحرام إن شاء الله آمنين قيل هذا ليس لكم لأنا قد بينا تحقيق الشك على مذهبكم ثم لم يكن لكم الاحتجاج بخروج عن موضع الشك ولو كنتم كذلك فقد ذكر الله أهل اليقين في غير موضع باسم القطع فقولوا لا يتم بلا تنيا ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم يقال قد ذكر الله تعالى الظن ولعل وعسى والخوف في موضع اليقين فقولوا عند السؤال نظن ونخاف ولعل ومثل ذا فإذ لم يجب هذا بما العرف فيه غيره وإن اعترض في مواضع لهذه الأحرف مساغ في حق اليقين مثل لعلك وكذلك أمر الثنيا ثم يعارض بجميع ما ذكر من الإيمان بالله
Then Allah Almighty said: 'O you who have believed' in more than one place with a definitive name, it is not permissible that one deserve anything of what the address conveys of command, prohibition, promise, threat, encouragement, and deterrence, so that the majority of the verses of Allah in the address would come out as mere play, while the truth among the schools is that of the one for whom this statement is not necessary, whether he said it or not. And Allah grants success. If someone says: 'Allah has mentioned the world (al-dunya) in more than one place of doubt, so the exception (al-thunya) is permissible on that, and also His saying: "You will enter the Sacred Mosque, if Allah wills, in safety." [48:27]' It is said: 'This is not for you, because we have already explained the verification of doubt according to your madhhab, and then you have no argument by departing from the place of doubt, even if you were like that. For Allah has mentioned the people of certainty in more than one place with a definitive name, so say that it is not complete without exception. And there is no power except with Allah. Then it is said: Allah Almighty has mentioned "zann" (conjecture), "la'alla" (perhaps), "asa" (maybe), and "khawf" (fear) in the context of certainty, so say when asked: "we conjecture," "we fear," "perhaps," and the like. So if this is not necessary for you, while custom has it otherwise - although in some places these letters have room in the case of certainty, like "la'alla" - then likewise the matter of al-thunya. Then it is refuted by all that has been mentioned about faith in Allah.'
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. First, the author states that Allah addresses believers with a definitive name ('O you who have believed') in many places, implying certainty. If one allows exception, it would make these verses meaningless. Then an objector argues that Allah Himself uses the word 'illa' (except) in some contexts, and also says 'if Allah wills' regarding entering the mosque, so exception is permissible. The refutation is that this argument does not apply to the objector because his own madhhab establishes doubt as a principle. Furthermore, Allah uses words like 'zann' (conjecture) and 'la'alla' (perhaps) in contexts of certainty, but the objector does not use those words in his own speech. Therefore, the use of such words in the Qur'an in certain contexts does not justify using them in faith, which requires certainty. The passage concludes that the objection is refuted by all that was previously mentioned about faith in Allah.

Key Terms
الثنيا (al-thunya)
الاستثناء
الشك
اليقين
ظن
لعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the Qur'an says 'in sha Allah' (if Allah wills) in some verses, it is permissible to say 'I am a believer if Allah wills' regarding one's faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the objector's own principle of doubt nullifies the analogy, and that God's use of such words in other contexts (like worldly matters) does not imply doubt in the reality of faith. Faith must be stated with certainty.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to the objection that the Qur'an uses 'istithna' in some verses?
A) He accepts the objection and changes his position.
B) He says the objector's own principle of doubt nullifies the analogy, and God's use of such words does not imply doubt in faith.
C) He says the verses are abrogated.
D) He remains silent.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the scriptural justification for istithna in faith by showing that the objector's own principles and the Qur'an's usage of tentative words in certainty contexts do not support its application to the definitive nature of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer,' do you sometimes add 'if Allah wills' in your heart? What does that hesitation reveal about your certainty in the fundamental truths of Islam? Would you hesitate to affirm that Allah is One?
?
Maturidi warns that allowing exception in faith reduces divine commands to 'mere play.' Reflect on your own speech: have you used phrases like 'I’ll try to be a good Muslim' instead of declaring commitment? How can you align your words with the definitive certainty that Allah demands?
?
The objector used a Qur’anic verse to justify his doubt. Are there any verses you twist to excuse your own spiritual shortcomings? What would change if you accepted Allah’s commands and promises without exception?

Hadith of Jibril and the Distinction Between Iman and Islam
Maturidi cites the Hadith of Jibril to prove the distinction between iman and islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi invokes the Hadith of Jibril to establish a clear distinction between iman and islam based on textual and rational arguments.

Core Idea
The agreement of Qur'an, Sunna, and Jibril proves iman and islam are distinct.

Maturidi turns to a well-known hadith to settle a theological debate about the relationship between iman and islam. In this passage, he presents the hadith and then argues that the very structure of the questioning shows that iman and islam are not identical. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping the Maturidi position on faith and practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the definitions of iman and islam given by the Prophet. Notice that they are presented as separate answers to separate questions.
2
Identify Maturidi's proof elements
After the hadith, Maturidi lists several evidences: the Book, the Sunna, Jibril's confirmation, the naming of acts, the Prophet's statement about Jibril teaching religion, and the impossibility of agreement on error. Count them and note how each supports the distinction.
There are six elements in the argument.
3
Understand the 'trustworthy ones' argument
Reflect on the final statement: it is impossible for the trustworthy ones of heaven (Jibril) and earth (the Prophet) to agree on teaching a distinction if the truth were unity. This is a logical argument from authority.
This underscores the strength of the proof.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why this distinction matters. Maturidi is countering those who conflate iman and islam. This hadith serves as a key proof for the Maturidi school's position that faith includes inner belief while Islam includes outward submission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جبريل فيما سأل رسول الله عن الإيمان فقال: «أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله واليوم الآخر والقدر خيره وشره من الله». وسأل عن الإسلام فقال: «أن تشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وتقيم الصلاة وتؤدى الزكاة وتصوم رمضان وتحج البيت». فقال في الأول: «فإن فعلت هذا فأنا مؤمن؟»، وفي الثاني: «فأنا مسلم؟» قال: «نعم، صدقت». قالوا: ففرق الكتاب بين الأمرين، ثم السنة، ثم تصديق جبريل في ذلك، ثم الشهادة بالاسم الذي ذلك فعله، ثم أخبر عليه السلام أن «هذا جبريل أتاكم ليعلمكم أمر دينكم». ولا يحتمل اجتماع أمناء السماء والأرض على تعليم أمر بالتفريق والحق فيها الجمع؛ فثبت به التفريق بينهما.
Jibril asked the Messenger of Allah about iman, and he said: 'That you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, the Last Day, and the divine decree, its good and its evil, from Allah.' And he asked about islam, and he said: 'That you testify that there is no god but Allah, establish prayer, give zakah, fast Ramadan, and pilgrimage to the House.' And he said in the first: 'If I do this, am I a believer?' and in the second: 'Am I a Muslim?' He said: 'Yes, you have spoken truth.' They said: The Book differentiated between the two matters, then the Sunna, then the confirmation of Jibril in that, then the testimony by the name by which that act is done, then he (peace be upon him) informed that 'This is Jibril, he came to you to teach you the matter of your religion.' And it is not possible that the trustworthy ones of heaven and earth would agree upon teaching something by differentiation while the truth in it is unity; so the differentiation between them is established.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a proof-text argument. Maturidi cites the well-known hadith of Jibril in which the Prophet defines iman and islam separately. The author argues that since the Qur'an (implied by 'the Book'), the Sunna, and Jibril himself (through his questioning) all treat iman and islam as distinct, and since the Prophet explicitly says this teaches the religion, it cannot be that they are the same. The phrase 'the trustworthy ones of heaven and earth' refers to Jibril (heaven) and the Prophet (earth). Their agreement on teaching a differentiation implies that the truth is indeed differentiation, not amalgamation. Thus the hadith establishes a distinction between iman and islam.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الإسلام
جبريل
التفريق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Jibril implies that iman and islam are identical because both are parts of the religion.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the very fact that the Prophet gave separate definitions and answered separate questions shows they are distinct, and the agreement of authorities confirms this.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument in the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the evidences he lists for the distinction between iman and islam?
A) The Book (Qur'an) differentiates between them
B) The Sunna differentiates between them
C) Jibril's confirmation
D) The consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Through the Hadith of Jibril and rational argument, Maturidi establishes that iman and islam are distinct realities, a foundational principle for Maturidi theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your inner belief (iman) and outward actions (islam) are the same thing? When you perform the prayers, do you also examine whether your heart truly believes in the six articles? This lesson demands you separate them. Which one are you neglecting today? Will you commit to checking your iman separately from your islam?
?
If Jibril himself came to teach the distinction between iman and islam, then ignoring that distinction is ignoring the teaching of the trustworthy ones. Which of the six proofs speaks directly to your confusion? Ask yourself: have you ever conflated the two out of laziness? What will you do today to honor the distinction in your own practice?
?
The Prophet said Jibril came to teach 'the matter of your religion.' This implies that understanding the difference is essential, not optional. Are you treating your deen as a single blur or as a structure with distinct pillars? If you were to die tonight, would you be more confident in your islam (acts) or your iman (belief)? Answer honestly and plan one concrete step to strengthen the weaker one.

Two Opinions on Iman and Islam
Maturidi presents and critiques two opinions on the scope of islam relative to iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on the relationship between iman and islam among those who define iman as tasdiq only, and the scriptural evidence each uses.

Core Idea
Iman is inner, Islam can be outer—but which view is more scriptural?

This passage continues the discussion on the definition of iman. Here, Maturidi presents two opinions among those who hold that iman is solely tasdiq. One opinion distinguishes islam as outward works, the other conflates them. Maturidi evaluates which is closer to the evidence from the Qur'an and Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the earlier discussion defined iman as tasdiq (affirmation) only. The scholars who hold this view now differ on the meaning of islam.
This passage builds on the previous definition.
2
Identify the first opinion
The first opinion holds that islam is a name for outward acts of obedience (qurub), while iman is specifically inner tasdiq. Support comes from the Qur'an allowing Bedouins to be called Muslims but not believers, and the hadith of Jibril.
Notice the evidence: Qur'anic permission to Bedouins and the report (hadith).
3
Identify the second opinion
The second opinion (attributed to 'the first ones') holds that islam applies to both outward and inward together, not distinguishing between them.
This opinion is critiqued by Maturidi.
4
Examine the critique
Maturidi argues that the second opinion contradicts the evidence it itself adduces, because when asked about iman, its proponents would attribute it to all good deeds. He says the first opinion is closer to the apparent meaning of the sources.
Maturidi's own view is not explicitly stated here, but he leans toward the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلف الذين قالوا: «الإيمان هو التصديق لا غير» في الإسلام. فمنهم من يوافق هؤلاء في جعل الإسلام اسما لما ظهر من القرب، والإيمان للتصديق خاصة، استدلالا بالذي ذكرت من حكم الكتاب والسنة: إنه أذن للأعراب بالتسمى بالإسلام بالظاهر ولم يأذن بالتسمى بالإيمان لما لم يكن لهم حقيقة في القلب. ومثله الخبر، إذ رد الإسلام إلى ظواهر الأمور والإيمان إلى التصديق بالذي ذكر. وهذا القول أقرب إلى ظاهر القولين من الأول؛ لأن الأولين لم يجعلوا اسم الإسلام على الظاهر والإيمان على التصديق، بل جعلوا الإسلام على الظاهر والباطن جميعا، فهم خالفوا جميع ما احتجوا به مع ما كان كل منهم إذا سئل عن الإيمان أضافه إلى جميع الخيرات. فعلى قولهم خالفوا ما احتجوا به من القرآن ببيان الموضع له وبما جاء من تفسير الأمناء في ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then those who said 'iman is tasdiq only' differed concerning islam. Among them are those who agree with these in making islam a name for what is apparent of acts of obedience, and iman specifically for tasdiq, using as evidence what I mentioned of the ruling of the Book and the Sunna: that He permitted the Bedouins to be called by islam outwardly but did not permit them to be called by iman because they had no reality in the heart. And similar is the report, for it attributed islam to outward matters and iman to tasdiq as mentioned. And this opinion is closer to the apparent meaning of the two opinions than the first; because the first ones did not make the name of islam for the outward and iman for tasdiq, but rather they made islam for both outward and inward together, so they contradicted all that they adduced as evidence, along with the fact that each of them, when asked about iman, would attribute it to all good deeds. So according to their opinion, they contradicted what they adduced as evidence from the Qur'an by clarifying its place and by what came of the interpretation of the trustworthy ones in that matter. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques two opinions among those who define iman as only tasdiq (affirmation). The first opinion, which the author implicitly favours, holds that islam is the name for outward acts of worship (qurub), while iman is specifically inner tasdiq. Its proponents cite the fact that the Qur'an allowed Bedouins to be called Muslims (by their outward submission) but not believers, because their hearts lacked true iman. They also cite a report (the hadith of Jibril) that attributes islam to outward matters and iman to tasdiq. The second opinion, attributed to 'the first ones', holds that islam applies to both outward and inward together, thus not distinguishing between them. The author argues that the second opinion contradicts the evidence from the Qur'an and the interpretations of the trustworthy (Jibril and the Prophet), and that the first opinion is closer to the apparent meaning of the sources. The passage ends with a pious phrase.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الإسلام
التصديق
الظاهر
الباطن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the first opinion is Maturidi's own view, but here he only presents two opinions and criticizes the second; his own view comes later.
Correction: Maturidi is simply evaluating which opinion is more consistent with the evidence; he does not explicitly state his own position in this passage.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the first opinion use to support the distinction between iman and islam?
A) The hadith of Jibril and the permission for Bedouins to be called Muslims
B) The consensus of scholars
C) The linguistic meaning of the words
D) The actions of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The more scripturally consistent view distinguishes islam as outward submission from iman as inner affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as a Muslim, do you pause to examine whether your heart truly affirms what your tongue declares? How often do you check the state of your inner tasdiq rather than resting on outward labels?
?
The Bedouins were allowed to be called Muslims outwardly but not believers because their hearts lacked true iman. Is there any area of your life where you settle for outward compliance while your heart is distant? What would it take for you to seek inner iman in that area?
?
The second opinion conflated iman with all good deeds, measuring faith by actions. Have you ever judged your own faith—or someone else's—based on visible deeds rather than inner conviction? How can you realign your understanding with the distinction taught in this lesson?

First Interpretation: Narrator Omission
Maturidi presents the first possible interpretation: narrator omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first interpretation of the Hadith of Jibril, which suggests that the narrator may have omitted part of the question, creating an apparent discrepancy.

Core Idea
A narrator's omission can cause an apparent contradiction in hadith.

This lesson explores the first of two ways Maturidi reconciles the famous Hadith of Jibril with a narration from Ibn Umar. The key insight is that the well-known version might lack the full question because the narrator did not transmit it. Maturidi uses Ibn Umar's report as evidence that the full question was indeed asked, thus resolving the conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that Maturidi starts by stating there is a disagreement in wording.
Focus on the first part: the report from Ibn Umar and the first interpretation.
2
Identify the core claim
Maturidi says the first report can be interpreted as the narrator not hearing the full question or omitting it in transmission.
Look for the phrase 'إما على أن الراوي لم يسمع الشرائع في السؤال'.
3
Examine the evidence
Maturidi supports this interpretation with Ibn Umar's narration and another hadith where 'the matter of your religion' is mentioned.
Ibn Umar's report serves as a key piece of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر الاختلاف الذي نشأ من خبر جبريل عليه السلام؛ قد روي في ذلك اختلاف في اللفظ. روي عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن الإيمان، ثم عن شرائع الإسلام، فأجاب بالذي ذكر في السؤال عن الإسلام. فيكون هذا الخبر تفسيرا للخبر الأول. ويحمل الخبر الأول على جهتين: إما على أن الراوي لم يسمع الشرائع في السؤال أو في الرواية عن الذي رواه، فروى كذلك، يؤيده خبر ابن عمر أن ذلك قد كان. ومن البعيد أن يكون مقدار الأول. يؤيده ابن عمر، لما يسقط الشهادة بما يخبر عن جبريل وعن الرسول بغير الذي قالا. وغير مدفوع حقا البعض على بعض، فيرويه على ما وقع عنده. وأيد هذا ما قد ذكر في بعض الأخبار أن النبي عليه السلام قال: «هذا جبريل أتاكم ليعلمكم دينكم»، وروي في غيره ليعلمكم أمر دينكم، فكان في الخبر «أمر دينكم» وإن خفي على الآخر، فمثله الخبر الأول. والجهة الثانية على أن الاكتفاء أحدهما به بوجهين: أنهم قد علموا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون مؤمنا غير مسلم في الحقيقة، أو مسلما غير مؤمن، فرأوا أن ذلك القدر كاف عن الإبلاغ في الذكر لظهور ذلك. والآخر أن يكون الثاني عنده البيان عن أفعال الإسلام يرويه باسمه، على مجاز اللغة في تسمية الشيء باسم سببه واسم المتصل به. ثم قد ثبت أنهما واحد في التحقيق على ما جرى به أحكام القرآن.
Then we mention the disagreement that arose from the report of Jibril (peace be upon him); indeed a difference in wording has been narrated concerning that. It was narrated from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both) that the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) was asked about faith, then about the laws of Islam, and he answered with what was mentioned in the question about Islam. So this report becomes an interpretation of the first report. And the first report is carried upon two aspects: Either that the narrator did not hear the laws in the question or in the narration from the one who narrated it, so he narrated it thus, and the report of Ibn Umar supports that this indeed happened. And it is unlikely that it was only the first [type]. Ibn Umar supports it, because he would invalidate testimony by reporting from Jibril and from the Messenger something other than what they said. And one part is not truly refuted by another part, so he narrates it as it occurred to him. And this is supported by what has been mentioned in some reports that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'This is Jibril who came to teach you your religion,' and it was narrated in another [report] 'to teach you the matter of your religion,' so the expression 'the matter of your religion' appeared in the report, even if it was hidden from the other [narrator], so the first report is similar to that. And the second aspect is that sufficing with one of them has two facets: First, they knew that one cannot [truly] be a believer without being a Muslim in reality, nor a Muslim without being a believer, so they saw that that amount was sufficient for conveying in the mention because of its obviousness. And second, that the second [narrator] had in his possession the explanation of the actions of Islam and narrated it under its name, as a linguistic metaphor of naming a thing by the name of its cause and the name of what is connected to it. Then it has been established that they are one in reality according to what the rulings of the Qur'an entail.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential contradiction between two hadith narrations: the well-known Hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet distinguishes between Islam, Iman, and Ihsan, and a narration from Ibn Umar where the Prophet was asked about Iman and then about the laws of Islam, but answered with the description of Islam. Maturidi presents two hermeneutical approaches to reconcile them. The first approach suggests that the narrator of the first hadith may not have heard the full question or omitted part, supported by Ibn Umar's explicit report that the question included both Iman and Islam but the answer was about Islam. The second approach argues that the Prophet's answer sufficed because the audience already knew that Iman and Islam are inseparable in reality—one cannot be a true believer without being a Muslim. Additionally, the second narrator (Ibn Umar) might have reported the actions of Islam under the name of Iman as a linguistic convention, where a thing is called by the name of its cause or something connected to it. Maturidi concludes that the two terms are essentially one in reality, as the Quranic rulings show.

Key Terms
الخبر الأول
الخبر الثاني
الراوي
الاختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first possible reason for the difference between the Hadith of Jibril and Ibn Umar's narration?
A) The Prophet changed His answer over time.
B) The narrator of the first hadith might have omitted part of the question.
C) Ibn Umar's narration is weak and should be abandoned.
D) The two hadiths are contradictory and cannot be reconciled.

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation resolves the apparent contradiction by suggesting the narrator of the common hadith omitted the full question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you transmit knowledge—whether in teaching, conversation, or even repeating a story—do you ever omit details that seem unimportant, only to realize later they changed the meaning? What might this reveal about your responsibility to convey the full truth? How can you ensure you do not omit what is essential?
?
Maturidi uses the Ibn Umar report to resolve an apparent contradiction, rather than rejecting one report. Reflect on a recent disagreement where you assumed inconsistency or contradiction. Did you seek to harmonize, or did you dismiss the other view? What would change if you approached all contradictions with the assumption that both sides might be true if fully understood?
?
The passage warns that 'one part is not truly refuted by another part'. Think of a relationship—with a spouse, child, or friend—where you have dismissed their perspective because it seemed to contradict yours. How can you humbly search for the omitted 'part' that might reconcile your differences?
